Unseen Matters

Scripture as the Foundation for Bodily Resurrection
Declares scripture as the relied-upon proof and refutes Mu'tazila rational argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author holds that bodily resurrection is proven by scripture rather than reason, and how the Mu'tazila rational argument is refuted.

Core Idea
Scripture, not reason, is the reliable proof for bodily resurrection.

In this passage, the author firmly states that the relied-upon evidence for bodily resurrection is scriptural revelation. He then presents the Mu'tazila's claim that reason proves its obligation, and systematically dismantles their argument by exposing its flawed premises. This lesson walks through that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
The author declares that the relied-upon proof is scripture. Notice how he prioritizes Islam, the Quran, and the Prophet as the clearest sources.
This sets the methodological foundation.
2
Examine the Mu'tazila rational argument
Identify the Mu'tazila claim: reward and punishment are obligatory upon God, and these require returning the exact bodies. This is based on their principle of obligation.
3
Analyze the author's objections
The author points out that the argument is built on a corrupt principle (asluhum al-fasid) and that a spiritual return is insufficient. The Mu'tazila counter that the obedient/disobedient is the body, not just the soul.
4
Understand the author's final answer
The author distinguishes real versus apparent consideration. In reality, the soul is the agent. Apparent consideration would require returning all parts from life, which Mu'tazila don't claim. Thus, it is better to adhere to scripture.
This concludes the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال لنا المعتمد في إثبات حشر الأجساد دليل السمع، والمفصح عنه غاية الإفصاح من الأديان دين الإسلام، ومن الكتب القرآن، ومن الأنبياء محمد . والمعتزلة يدعون إثباته بل وجوبه بدليل العقل وتقريره أنه يجب على الله ثواب المطيعين ، وعقاب العاصين، وأعواض المستحقين ولا يتأتى ذلك إلا بإعادتهم بأعيانهم ، فيجب . لأن ما لا يتأتى الواجب إلا به واجب. وربما يتمسكون بهذا في وجوب الإعادة على تقدير الفناء، ومبناه على أصلهم الفاسد في الوجوب على الله تعالى، وفي كون ترك الجزاء ظلماً لا يصح صدوره من الله تعالى، مع إمكان المناقشة في أن الواجب لا يتم إلا به ، وأنه لا يكفي المعاد الروحاني. ويدفعون ذلك بأن المطيع والعاصي هي هذه الجملة أو الأجزاء الأصلية، لا الروح وحده . فلا يصل الجزاء إلى مستحقه إلا بإعادتها . والجواب - أنه إن اعتبر الأمر بحسب الحقيقة، فالمستحق هو الروح، لأن مبنى الطاعة والعصيان على الإدراكات والإرادات والأفعال والحركات، وهو المبدأ للكل. وإن اعتبر بحسب الظاهر، يلزم أن يعاد جميع الأجزاء الكائنة من أول التكليف إلى الممات. ولا يقولون بذلك، فالأولى التمسك بدليل السمع، وتقريره أن الحشر والإعادة أمر ممكن أخبر به الصادق فيكون واقعاً، أما الإمكان فلأن الكلام فيما عدم بعد الوجود، أو تفرق بعد الاجتماع، أو مات بعد الحياة، فيكون قائلاً لذلك. والفاعل هو الله القادر على كل الممكنات العالم بجميع والجزئيات. وأما الإخبار فلما تواتر من الأنبياء، سيما نبينا أنهم كانوا يقولون بذلك، ولما ورد في القرآن من نصوص لا يحتمل أكثرها التأويل مثل قوله تعالى : قال من يحيي العظام وهي رميم قل يحييها الذي أنشأها أول مرة فإذا هم من الأجداث إلى ربهم ينسلون فسيقولون من يعيدنا قل الذي فطركم أول مرة أيحسب الإنسان أن لن نجمع عظامه بلى قادرين على أن نسوي بنانه وقالوا لجلودهم لم شهدتم علينا قالوا أنطقنا الله الذي أنطق كل شيء كلما نضجت جلودهم بدلناهم جلوداً غيرها يوم تشقق الأرض عنهم سراعاً ذلك حشر علينا يسير أفلا يعلم إذا بعثر ما في القبور
He said: The relied-upon [proof] in establishing the resurrection of bodies is the evidence of scripture, and the most clearly expressing of religions is the religion of Islam, of books is the Qur'an, and of prophets is Muhammad. The Mu'tazila claim to prove it, nay, its obligation by rational proof, and its establishment: that reward for the obedient and punishment for the disobedient and compensations for those entitled are obligatory upon God, and that cannot be achieved except by returning them as themselves, so it is obligatory. Because that without which the obligatory cannot be achieved is obligatory. And perhaps they cling to this regarding the obligation of return upon the assumption of annihilation, and it is based on their corrupt principle of obligation upon God Most High, and on the notion that leaving out recompense is injustice, the occurrence of which from God Most High is not permissible, while it is possible to object that the obligatory is not completed except by it, and that the spiritual return is not sufficient. And they repel that by [saying] that the obedient and the disobedient are this whole or the original parts, not the soul alone. So recompense does not reach its deserving except by returning them. The answer: If the matter is considered according to reality, then the deserving is the soul, because the basis of obedience and disobedience is perceptions, volitions, actions, and movements, and it is the principle of all. And if considered according to appearance, it would require that all the parts that came into being from the beginning of obligation until death be returned. And they do not say that. So it is better to adhere to the evidence of scripture, and its exposition: that the gathering and return is a possible matter which the truthful one has informed of, so it will occur. As for possibility, because the discourse is about something that disappeared after existence, or dispersed after assembly, or died after life, so it is said accordingly. And the agent is God, powerful over all possibles, knowing all universals and particulars. As for the information, due to the mutawatir [reports] from the prophets, especially our prophet, that they used to say that, and due to what has come in the Qur'an of texts, most of which do not admit interpretation, such as His statement: 'Who will give life to bones while they are decayed?' Say, 'He will give them life who produced them the first time.' Then behold, they from the graves to their Lord will hasten. They will say, 'Who will return us?' Say, 'He who created you the first time.' Does man think that We will not assemble his bones? Yes, [We are] able to proportion his fingertips. They will say to their skins, 'Why did you testify against us?' They will say, 'We were made to speak by Allah, who has made everything speak.' Whenever their skins are roasted, We will replace them with other skins. On the Day the earth is split from them rapidly; that is a gathering easy for Us. Does he not know when what is in the graves is scattered?
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents the author's definitive position on the proof of bodily resurrection. It is a classification and argument: it first states the correct methodology (scripture as the relied-upon proof), then refutes the Mu'tazila rational argument, and finally provides the scriptural proof. The passage opens by affirming that the reliable evidence (al-mu'tamad) for bodily resurrection is scriptural (dali al-sam'), with Islam, the Qur'an, and Prophet Muhammad as its clearest expressions. The Mu'tazila, by contrast, claim to prove it—even its obligation—by reason. Their argument: reward and punishment are obligatory upon God, and these cannot be realized without returning the identical individuals; therefore, such return is obligatory, as the means to an obligatory end is itself obligatory. The author notes that this is based on the Mu'tazila's corrupt principle (asluhum al-fasid) that obligations are binding on God and that leaving out recompense would be injustice unbecoming of God. Moreover, the author objects that the necessary means is not fulfilled by this argument, and that a purely spiritual return is insufficient. The Mu'tazila parry by insisting that the obedient or disobedient is the whole body or the original parts, not the soul alone, so recompense requires returning these. The author's answer distinguishes between a real (haqiqi) and an apparent (zahiri) consideration. In reality, the deserving entity is the soul, because the basis of obedience and disobedience lies in perceptions, volitions, actions, and movements—all originating from the soul. If we consider the apparent sense, it would require returning all parts from the start of obligation to death, which the Mu'tazila themselves do not affirm. Therefore, it is better to adhere to scripture. The author then expounds the scriptural proof: bodily resurrection is a possible matter (mumkin) that the truthful (the prophets) have informed of, so it will certainly occur. Possibility is established because the subject is something that has existed, then ceased or disintegrated, and God is all-powerful and all-knowing. The information comes from the mutawatir reports of the prophets, especially our Prophet, and from unequivocal Qur'anic verses, which the author then lists: verses about reviving bones, emergence from graves, return of the same Creator, assembly of bones and fingertips, speaking skins, replacement of roasted skins, the earth splitting open, and the scattering of graves. These texts are presented as decisive proof that bodily resurrection is a reality.

Key Terms
المعتمد
دليل السمع
المعتزلة
وجوب الإعادة
المعاد الروحاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the Mu'tazila rational proof for bodily resurrection is valid and sufficient.
Correction: The author shows that their argument is based on a flawed principle of obligation upon God and that it does not successfully prove the necessity of bodily return. The proper proof is scriptural.

Checkpoint
What is the author's primary reason for preferring scriptural evidence over rational proof for bodily resurrection?
A) The rational proof is too complicated
B) The rational proof is based on a corrupt principle of obligation upon God
C) The rational proof does not mention the soul
D) The rational proof requires returning all parts

Key Takeaway
Scripture, not flawed rational arguments, is the reliable foundation for believing in bodily resurrection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine your resurrection, do you rely more on rational proofs you’ve constructed or on the plain verses of the Quran? Which one actually settles your doubts when they arise?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed God owes reward to the obedient. Have you ever acted righteously while secretly feeling that God now owes you something? How does calling that principle 'corrupt' change your understanding of worship?
?
If the soul alone is the true recipient of reward, why do you still care about your body being resurrected? What does your attachment to your body reveal about your trust in God’s wisdom and promise?

The Scriptural Proof: Possibility and Prophetic Reports
Establishes the possibility of bodily resurrection and provides scriptural evidence from the Qur'an.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural argument for bodily resurrection: its possibility, the reliable reports from prophets, and the decisive Qur'anic verses.

Core Idea
Bodily resurrection is possible and confirmed by the truthful prophets and clear Qur'anic texts.

Having refuted the rational argument, the author now presents the positive scriptural proof. He first establishes the possibility of bodily resurrection, then adduces the mutawatir reports of the prophets and specific Qur'anic verses that explicitly describe the return of bodies. This lesson unpacks that scriptural case.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure of the scriptural proof
The author breaks it into two parts: establishing the possibility of resurrection, and presenting the actual information from prophets and the Quran.
2
Examine the argument for possibility
Bodily resurrection is possible because it concerns something that existed and then ceased, and God is all-powerful and all-knowing. This is a standard theological argument.
The author uses 'mumkin' (possible) as the modality.
3
Review the prophetic reports
The author mentions the mutawatir (mass-transmitted) reports from the prophets, especially Prophet Muhammad, that they preached bodily resurrection.
4
Read the Qur'anic verses
The author cites several verses that unequivocally affirm bodily resurrection. Identify each verse and note how it reinforces the doctrine.
The verses are given in concatenated form but each is a separate proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال لنا المعتمد في إثبات حشر الأجساد دليل السمع، والمفصح عنه غاية الإفصاح من الأديان دين الإسلام، ومن الكتب القرآن، ومن الأنبياء محمد . والمعتزلة يدعون إثباته بل وجوبه بدليل العقل وتقريره أنه يجب على الله ثواب المطيعين ، وعقاب العاصين، وأعواض المستحقين ولا يتأتى ذلك إلا بإعادتهم بأعيانهم ، فيجب . لأن ما لا يتأتى الواجب إلا به واجب. وربما يتمسكون بهذا في وجوب الإعادة على تقدير الفناء، ومبناه على أصلهم الفاسد في الوجوب على الله تعالى، وفي كون ترك الجزاء ظلماً لا يصح صدوره من الله تعالى، مع إمكان المناقشة في أن الواجب لا يتم إلا به ، وأنه لا يكفي المعاد الروحاني. ويدفعون ذلك بأن المطيع والعاصي هي هذه الجملة أو الأجزاء الأصلية، لا الروح وحده . فلا يصل الجزاء إلى مستحقه إلا بإعادتها . والجواب - أنه إن اعتبر الأمر بحسب الحقيقة، فالمستحق هو الروح، لأن مبنى الطاعة والعصيان على الإدراكات والإرادات والأفعال والحركات، وهو المبدأ للكل. وإن اعتبر بحسب الظاهر، يلزم أن يعاد جميع الأجزاء الكائنة من أول التكليف إلى الممات. ولا يقولون بذلك، فالأولى التمسك بدليل السمع، وتقريره أن الحشر والإعادة أمر ممكن أخبر به الصادق فيكون واقعاً، أما الإمكان فلأن الكلام فيما عدم بعد الوجود، أو تفرق بعد الاجتماع، أو مات بعد الحياة، فيكون قائلاً لذلك. والفاعل هو الله القادر على كل الممكنات العالم بجميع والجزئيات. وأما الإخبار فلما تواتر من الأنبياء، سيما نبينا أنهم كانوا يقولون بذلك، ولما ورد في القرآن من نصوص لا يحتمل أكثرها التأويل مثل قوله تعالى : قال من يحيي العظام وهي رميم قل يحييها الذي أنشأها أول مرة فإذا هم من الأجداث إلى ربهم ينسلون فسيقولون من يعيدنا قل الذي فطركم أول مرة أيحسب الإنسان أن لن نجمع عظامه بلى قادرين على أن نسوي بنانه وقالوا لجلودهم لم شهدتم علينا قالوا أنطقنا الله الذي أنطق كل شيء كلما نضجت جلودهم بدلناهم جلوداً غيرها يوم تشقق الأرض عنهم سراعاً ذلك حشر علينا يسير أفلا يعلم إذا بعثر ما في القبور
He said: The relied-upon [proof] in establishing the resurrection of bodies is the evidence of scripture, and the most clearly expressing of religions is the religion of Islam, of books is the Qur'an, and of prophets is Muhammad. The Mu'tazila claim to prove it, nay, its obligation by rational proof, and its establishment: that reward for the obedient and punishment for the disobedient and compensations for those entitled are obligatory upon God, and that cannot be achieved except by returning them as themselves, so it is obligatory. Because that without which the obligatory cannot be achieved is obligatory. And perhaps they cling to this regarding the obligation of return upon the assumption of annihilation, and it is based on their corrupt principle of obligation upon God Most High, and on the notion that leaving out recompense is injustice, the occurrence of which from God Most High is not permissible, while it is possible to object that the obligatory is not completed except by it, and that the spiritual return is not sufficient. And they repel that by [saying] that the obedient and the disobedient are this whole or the original parts, not the soul alone. So recompense does not reach its deserving except by returning them. The answer: If the matter is considered according to reality, then the deserving is the soul, because the basis of obedience and disobedience is perceptions, volitions, actions, and movements, and it is the principle of all. And if considered according to appearance, it would require that all the parts that came into being from the beginning of obligation until death be returned. And they do not say that. Commentary
This passage presents the author's definitive position on the proof of bodily resurrection. It is a classification and argument: it first states the correct methodology (scripture as the relied-upon proof), then refutes the Mu'tazila rational argument, and finally provides the scriptural proof. The passage opens by affirming that the reliable evidence (al-mu'tamad) for bodily resurrection is scriptural (dali al-sam'), with Islam, the Qur'an, and Prophet Muhammad as its clearest expressions. Their argument: reward and punishment are obligatory upon God, and these cannot be realized without returning the identical individuals; therefore, such return is obligatory, as the means to an obligatory end is itself obligatory. The author notes that this is based on the Mu'tazila's corrupt principle (asluhum al-fasid) that obligations are binding on God and that leaving out recompense would be injustice unbecoming of God. Moreover, the author objects that the necessary means is not fulfilled by this argument, and that a purely spiritual return is insufficient. The Mu'tazila parry by insisting that the obedient or disobedient is the whole body or the original parts, not the soul alone, so recompense requires returning these. The author's answer distinguishes between a real (haqiqi) and an apparent (zahiri) consideration. In reality, the deserving entity is the soul, because the basis of obedience and disobedience lies in perceptions, volitions, actions, and movements—all originating from the soul. If we consider the apparent sense, it would require returning all parts from the start of obligation to death, which the Mu'tazila themselves do not affirm. Therefore, it is better to adhere to scripture. The author then expounds the scriptural proof: bodily resurrection is a possible matter (mumkin) that the truthful (the prophets) have informed of, so it will certainly occur. Possibility is established because the subject is something that has existed, then ceased or disintegrated, and God is all-powerful and all-knowing. These texts are presented as decisive proof that bodily resurrection is a reality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that bodily resurrection is rationally impossible and must be interpreted metaphorically.
Correction: The author argues it is possible because God is all-powerful, and prophets and the Quran confirm it as a literal event.

Checkpoint
How does the author establish the possibility of bodily resurrection?
A) By arguing that it is a logical necessity
B) By noting that it concerns something that once existed and God can recreate it
C) By citing rational proofs from philosophy
D) By claiming that it is a miracle beyond reason

Key Takeaway
Bodily resurrection is possible and confirmed by mass-transmitted prophetic reports and clear Qur'anic verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imagine standing before your Creator with the very body you are using to read this lesson. How does the reality that every bone and fingertip will be recreated and held accountable change what you do with your body today? What action will you take to honor that truth?
?
You have just read that the prophets, especially Muhammad, repeatedly and emphatically announced bodily resurrection. If you truly believe their words are from God, why do you still act as if this life is the only one? Name one comfort or sin you are clinging to that you would abandon if you lived in certainty of the Day of Reckoning.
?
The Qur'anic verses cited emphasize the detailed recreation of even the fingertips. Do you live as if God's knowledge and power encompass every part of you, including the most private thoughts and deeds? What is one hidden thing you need to repent of right now, knowing it will be exposed on that day?

Unseen Matters
intermediate
8 min
Proof of Bodily Resurrection from Scripture
The author proves bodily resurrection by possibility and revelation, concluding it is a necessary of religion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that bodily resurrection is proven by revelation (al-sam‘) and that denying it constitutes disbelief (kufr).

Core Idea
Bodily resurrection is a revealed truth, not a rational impossibility.

How do we know that our bodies will be resurrected on the Last Day? Reason alone cannot settle the question. In this passage, the author navigates a philosophical objection about who deserves reward or punishment and then argues that resurrection is possible and that God has informed us of it through His prophets. The decisive proof comes from scripture, making belief in bodily resurrection a necessary part of Islamic faith.

Guided Path
1
Follow the philosophical objection
The passage begins by noting that the obedient and disobedient person is the whole body or its original parts, not just the soul. This poses a problem: if only the soul is the true subject, then reward or punishment of the body seems pointless. Read the opening lines to see how the author sets up this tension.
Consider why the author brings up this objection before presenting the proof.
2
Understand the two perspectives
The author offers two ways to consider the issue: in reality (ḥaqīqa), the soul is the deserving subject; in appearance (ẓāhir), all parts from obligation to death would need to be restored. Since the upholders of bodily resurrection do not demand that, the better path is to rely on revelation.
This is a key move: reason leads to a paradox, so scripture must decide.
3
Grasp the argument from possibility
The author then lays out the rational foundation: resurrection and return are possible matters because they involve something that ceased, dispersed, or died. God is all‑powerful and all‑knowing, so He can easily restore it.
Possibility is established without naming a specific mechanism.
4
Examine the scriptural evidence
The core proof is the widespread reports (tawātur) from the prophets and the clear Qur’anic verses that cannot be interpreted allegorically. Several verses are cited, each affirming bodily resurrection in explicit terms.
Notice the author uses multiple verses to show the strength of the evidence.
5
Learn the conclusion
The passage ends with a powerful statement: belief in resurrection is a necessary doctrine of the religion (ḍarūriyyāt al-dīn), and denying it is definitely disbelief (kufr).
This elevates the issue from a mere theological opinion to a matter of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك بأن المطيع والعاصي هي هذه الجملة أو الأجزاء الأصلية، لا الروح وحده . فلا يصل الجزاء إلى مستحقه إلا بإعادتها (۱) .
الكليات
والجواب - أنه إن اعتبر الأمر بحسب الحقيقة، فالمستحق هو الروح، لأن مبنى الطاعة والعصيان على الإدراكات والإرادات والأفعال والحركات، وهو المبدأ للكل. وإن اعتبر بحسب الظاهر، يلزم أن يعاد جميع الأجزاء الكائنة من أول التكليف إلى الممات. ولا يقولون بذلك، فالأولى التمسك بدليل السمع، وتقريره أن الحشر والإعادة أمر ممكن أخبر به الصادق فيكون واقعاً، أما الإمكان فلأن الكلام فيما عدم بعد الوجود، أو تفرق بعد الاجتماع، أو مات بعد الحياة، فيكون قائلاً لذلك. والفاعل هو الله القادر على كل الممكنات العالم بجميع والجزئيات. وأما الإخبار فلما تواتر من الأنبياء، سيما نبينا أنهم كانوا يقولون بذلك، ولما ورد في القرآن من نصوص لا يحتمل أكثرها التأويل مثل قوله تعالى : قال من يحيي العظام وهي رميم قل يحييها الذي أنشأها أول مرة) (٢) فإذا هم من الأجداث إلى ربهم ينسلون (۳) فسيقولون من يعيدنا قل الذي فطركم أول مرة (٤)، أيحسب الإنسان أن لن نجمع عظامه بلى قادرين على أن نسوي بنانه (٥) ،
وقالوا لجلودهم لم شهدتم علينا قالوا أنطقنا الله الذي أنطق كل شيء (٦) ، كلما نضجت جلودهم بدلناهم جلوداً غيرها ) (۷) ، يوم تشقق الأرض عنهم سراعاً ذلك حشر علينا يسير) (۸) ، أفلا يعلم إذا بعثر ما في القبور (۹)
إلى غير ذلك من الآيات، وفي الأحاديث ايضاً كثيرة. وبالجملة فإثبات الحشر من ضروريات الدين، وإنكاره كفر بيقين.
That is because the obedient and the disobedient are this whole or the original parts, not the soul alone. So the recompense does not reach its due except by restoring them (complete). General principles: And the answer - if the matter is considered in terms of reality, then the deserving one is the soul, because the basis of obedience and disobedience is perceptions, will, actions, and movements, and it is the principle of all. And if considered in terms of appearance, it would require that all the parts existing from the beginning of obligation to death be restored. And they do not say that. So it is better to adhere to the proof of revelation (al-sam‘): its determination is that the gathering (al-hashr) and return (al-i‘ada) are possible matters about which the truthful one informed, so they occur. As for possibility, because the discussion concerns something that ceased after existence, or dispersed after union, or died after life, so it is something that exists. And the agent is Allah, powerful over all possibles, knowing all universals and particulars. As for the report, it is due to the widespread reports from the prophets, especially our Prophet, that they used to say that, and due to what came in the Qur’an of texts most of which cannot be interpreted allegorically, such as His statement (Exalted): "Say: 'Who gives life to the bones when they are rotted?' Say: 'He will give them life who produced them the first time'" (Q 36:78-79); "Then behold, they from the graves to their Lord will hasten" (Q 36:51); "They will say: 'Who will return us?' Say: 'He who created you the first time'" (Q 17:51); "Does man think that We will not assemble his bones? Yes, We are able to proportion his fingertips" (Q 75:3-4); "And they will say to their skins: 'Why did you testify against us?' They will say: 'Allah, who gives speech to all things, gave us speech'" (Q 41:21); "Whenever their skins are roasted We will replace them with other skins" (Q 4:56); "On the day when the earth will split from them swiftly; that is a gathering easy for Us" (Q 50:44? actually Q 50:44 is similar, the text says "يوم تشقق الأرض عنهم سراعاً ذلك حشر علينا يسير" which is Q 50:44); "Does he not know when what is in the graves is scattered?" (Q 100:9). And besides these verses, and in the hadiths also many. And in summary, proving the gathering is from the necessities of religion, and denying it is definitely disbelief.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a proof of bodily resurrection (al-hashr al-jismānī) based on revelation (al-sam‘). It begins with a discussion of the identity of the person subject to reward and punishment: the obedient and disobedient person is the whole body or its original parts, not the soul alone. Therefore, recompense cannot reach its deserving recipient unless the whole body is restored. The author then considers two perspectives: in reality (ḥaqīqa), the soul is the true subject because it is the source of perceptions, will, and actions; but in appearance (ẓāhir), it would require the return of all parts from the beginning of obligation to death—a position the upholders of bodily resurrection do not hold. Hence, the proper course is to rely on the proof of revelation. The argument (taqrīr) is that resurrection and return are possible matters—the discussion concerns something that ceased to exist after existence, dispersed after union, or died after life, so it is a possible entity—and the agent is Allah, powerful over all possibles and knowing all universals and particulars. Then the report (khabar) comes from the widespread reports of the prophets, especially our Prophet, and from clear Qur’anic verses that cannot be interpreted allegorically. Several verses are cited, such as those speaking of reviving bones, resurrection from graves, and replacing skin. The passage concludes that proving the gathering (al-ḥashr) is among the necessary doctrines of the religion (ḍarūriyyāt al-dīn), and denying it is definitely disbelief (kufr).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think bodily resurrection is rationally impossible or unnecessary.
Correction: The passage shows resurrection is possible (since God is all‑powerful) and established by undeniable revelation, making belief obligatory.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

?

?

?

What practical step will you take tonight to bring your body into obedience to Allah, knowing that every limb will be held accountable?

Refutation of the Spiritual Interpretation of Resurrection Verses
Objection and answer regarding bodily resurrection verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that bodily resurrection verses cannot be allegorically interpreted in the same way as anthropomorphism verses, and the consequences of doing so.

Core Idea
Allegorical interpretation is only valid when the literal meaning is impossible, not merely difficult.

The objector claims that bodily resurrection verses are no more explicit than anthropomorphism verses, which required allegorical interpretation. Therefore, resurrection verses should also be interpreted spiritually. The author counters that the literal meaning of resurrection is not impossible, and allegorizing it would imply the prophets were lying to the common people.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and understand the analogy
The objector argues that resurrection verses are similar in apparent clarity to verses about anthropomorphism (tashbih) and compulsion (jabr), which were necessarily interpreted allegorically. Therefore, resurrection verses should also be interpreted allegorically as referring to spiritual resurrection.
2
Analyze the author's first response: the impossibility criterion
The author states that allegorical interpretation is only required when the apparent meaning is impossible (ta‘adhdhur al-ẓāhir). Since bodily resurrection is not impossible—especially if the restored body is like the original but not identical—the literal meaning remains valid.
3
Examine the second response: implication of prophetic lying
Interpreting resurrection verses as mere similes for the common people would attribute lying to the prophets, because the common people understand only the literal meanings, which according to the objector have no reality. This would mean the prophets intentionally misled most of humanity.
4
Note the concession about legitimate allegorical interpretation
The author concedes that if one holds that the apparent meanings, while real and intended, also serve as similes for deeper spiritual truths (as many Islamic scholars do), this is acceptable. The key is that the literal reality is not negated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الآيات المشعرة بالمعاد الجسماني ليست أكثر وأظهر من الآيات المشعرة بالتشبيه والجبر والقدر، ونحو ذلك، وقد وجب تأويلها قطعاً، فلنصرف هذه ايضاً إلى بيان المعاد الروحاني، وأحوال سعادة النفوس وشقاوتها بعد مفارقة الأبدان على وجه يفهمه العوام، فإن الأنبياء مبعوثون إلى كافة الخلائق لإرشادهم إلى سبيل الحق، وتكميل نفوسهم بحسب القوة النظرية والعملية، وتبقية النظام المفضي إلى صلاح الكل ، وذلك بالترغيب والترهيب بالوعد والوعيد والبشارة بما يعتقدونه لذة وكمالاً، والإنذار عما يعتقدونه ألماً ونقصاناً ، وأكثرهم عوام تقصر عقولهم عن فهم الكمالات الحقيقية ، واللذات العقلية، وتقتصر على ما لقوه من اللذات والآلام الحسية، وعرفوه من الكمالات والنقصانات البدنية، فوجب أن تخاطبهم الأنبياء بما هو مثال للمعاد الحقيقي ترغيباً وترهيباً للعوام، وتتميماً لأمر النظام، وهذا ما قال أبو نصر (۱) الفارابي أن الكلام مثل وخيالات للفلسفة. قلنا : إنما يجب التأويل عند تعذر الظاهر. ولا تعذر ههنا، سيما على القول يكون البدن المعاد مثل الأول، لاعينه ، وما ذكرتم من حمل كلام الأنبياء ونصوص الكتاب على الإشارة إلى مثال معاد النفس والرعاية لمصلحة العامة تسبة للأنبياء إلى الكذب فيما يتعلق بالتبليغ والقصد إلى تضليل أكثر الخلائق ، والتعصب طول العمر لترويج الباطل وإخفاء الحق لأنهم لا يفهمون إلا هذه الظواهر التي لا حقيقة لها عندكم . نعم ، لو قيل : إن هذه الظواهر مع إرادتها من الكلام وثبوتها في نفس الأمر مثل للمعاد الروحاني واللذات والآلام العقلية، وكذا أكثر ظواهر القرآن على ما يذكره المحققون من علماء الإسلام لكان حقاً لا ريب فيه ولا اعتداد بمن
ينميه .
For if it is said: The verses indicating bodily resurrection are not more numerous or clearer than the verses indicating anthropomorphism (tashbīh) and compulsion (jabr) and predetermination (qadar) and the like, and those necessarily required allegorical interpretation (ta‘wīl), so let us also turn these to explaining spiritual resurrection and the states of the souls’ happiness and misery after separation from bodies, in a way that the common people understand. For the prophets are sent to all creatures to guide them to the path of truth, to perfect their souls in terms of theoretical and practical power, and to preserve the order leading to the welfare of all. That is by encouragement and intimidation, promise and threat, glad tidings of what they consider pleasure and perfection, and warnings of what they consider pain and deficiency. Most of them are common people whose intellects fall short of understanding true perfections and intellectual pleasures, and are limited to what they have encountered of sensory pleasures and pains, and know of bodily perfections and deficiencies. So it is necessary that the prophets address them with that which is a similitude (mithāl) of the true resurrection, as encouragement and intimidation for the common people, and to complete the matter of order. This is what Abu Nasr al-Farabi said: that religious discourse is like similitudes and imaginations for philosophy. We say: Allegorical interpretation is only required when the apparent meaning is impossible (ta‘adhdhur al-ẓāhir). And here it is not impossible, especially on the view that the restored body is like the first, not identical. And what you mentioned of interpreting the speech of the prophets and the texts of the Book as reference to a similitude of the soul’s resurrection and care for the public interest attributes to the prophets lying (kidhb) concerning conveying and intending to mislead most creatures, and lifelong stubbornness to promote falsehood and conceal the truth, because they understand only these apparent meanings which, according to you, have no reality. Yes, if it is said that these apparent meanings, while intended in speech and established in themselves, are a similitude for spiritual resurrection and intellectual pleasures and pains, and likewise most apparent meanings of the Qur’an, as the investigators among the scholars of Islam mention, then that would be true without doubt, and no attention is paid to one who denies it.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues that the verses about bodily resurrection are no more numerous or clear than verses implying anthropomorphism (tashbīh), compulsion (jabr), and predetermination (qadar). Since those verses were necessarily interpreted allegorically (ta‘wīl), resurrection verses should also be interpreted to refer to spiritual resurrection. The objector further argues that prophets address common people in figurative language they can understand, using similes (mithāl) and images. This is attributed to al-Farabi, who said religious discourse is like similes and imaginations for philosophy. The author's answer (قلنا) makes several points: First, allegorical interpretation is only required when the apparent meaning is impossible (ta‘adhdhur al-ẓāhir). Here it is not impossible, especially if the restored body is similar (mithl) to the original, not identical. Second, interpreting prophetic speech and scriptural texts as mere similes for the soul's resurrection and as a means of social order implies that the prophets were lying (kidhb) and intending to mislead the majority of creatures, stubbornly promoting falsehood and hiding the truth—since the common people understand only these apparent meanings which, according to the objector, have no reality. Finally, the author concedes that if one holds that these apparent meanings, while genuinely intended and real in themselves, are similes for spiritual resurrection and intellectual pleasures and pains—as many Islamic scholars uphold—this is acceptable and not to be dismissed. The passage thus defends the literal reality of bodily resurrection while allowing an allegorical dimension in the sense of deeper spiritual truths.

Correction: Allegorical interpretation is only required when the literal meaning is impossible; bodily resurrection is not impossible, so its verses retain their literal meaning.

Key Takeaway

Defense of Bodily Resurrection
Al-Taftazani defends bodily resurrection against metaphorical interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani considers belief in bodily resurrection a necessity of religion and how he refutes the claim that it should be interpreted metaphorically.

Core Idea
Bodily resurrection is a necessary belief; interpreting it away undermines prophetic truthfulness.

Al-Taftazani tackles a profound challenge: if verses about bodily resurrection can be interpreted metaphorically, as some philosophers argue, then prophetic teachings lose their literal truth. He begins by affirming that belief in the Resurrection (al-hashr) is a necessary requirement of Islam. Then he presents and refutes the objection that such verses should be interpreted spiritually, like anthropomorphic verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening affirmation
Notice how al-Taftazani begins by stating that belief in resurrection is a necessity of religion and denial is clear disbelief. This sets the high stakes.
2
Understand the objection
The objector argues that bodily resurrection verses are like anthropomorphism verses (tashbih) which required interpretation. So why not interpret these spiritually? They claim prophets use sensory language for the masses.
This objection is based on al-Farabi's philosophy.
3
Examine al-Taftazani's response
He responds that interpretation is only needed when the literal meaning is impossible. Here it is not impossible. Moreover, to claim that prophets deliberately use false appearances is to accuse them of lying.
This is a strong refutation.
4
Reflect on the correct view
Al-Taftazani concludes by stating that the apparent meanings are true in themselves and also serve as examples for spiritual realities. This is the position of the verifiers among Islamic scholars.
This shows that literal and spiritual interpretations can coexist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى غير ذلك من الآيات، وفي الأحاديث ايضاً كثيرة. وبالجملة فإثبات الحشر من ضروريات الدين، وإنكاره كفر بيقين. فإن قيل : الآيات المشعرة بالمعاد الجسماني ليست أكثر وأظهر من الآيات المشعرة بالتشبيه والجبر والقدر، ونحو ذلك، وقد وجب تأويلها قطعاً، فلنصرف هذه ايضاً إلى بيان المعاد الروحاني، وأحوال سعادة النفوس وشقاوتها بعد مفارقة الأبدان على وجه يفهمه العوام، فإن الأنبياء مبعوثون إلى كافة الخلائق لإرشادهم إلى سبيل الحق، وتكميل نفوسهم بحسب القوة النظرية والعملية، وتبقية النظام المفضي إلى صلاح الكل ، وذلك بالترغيب والترهيب بالوعد والوعيد والبشارة بما يعتقدونه لذة وكمالاً، والإنذار عما يعتقدونه ألماً ونقصاناً ، وأكثرهم عوام تقصر عقولهم عن فهم الكمالات الحقيقية ، واللذات العقلية، وتقتصر على ما لقوه من اللذات والآلام الحسية، وعرفوه من الكمالات والنقصانات البدنية، فوجب أن تخاطبهم الأنبياء بما هو مثال للمعاد الحقيقي ترغيباً وترهيباً للعوام، وتتميماً لأمر النظام، وهذا ما قال أبو نصر (۱) الفارابي أن الكلام مثل وخيالات للفلسفة. قلنا : إنما يجب التأويل عند تعذر الظاهر. ولا تعذر ههنا، سيما على القول يكون البدن المعاد مثل الأول، لاعينه ، وما ذكرتم من حمل كلام الأنبياء ونصوص الكتاب على الإشارة إلى مثال معاد النفس والرعاية لمصلحة العامة نسبة للأنبياء إلى الكذب فيما يتعلق بالتبليغ والقصد إلى تضليل أكثر الخلائق ، والتعصب طول العمر لترويج الباطل وإخفاء الحق لأنهم لا يفهمون إلا هذه الظواهر التي لا حقيقة لها عندكم . نعم ، لو قيل : إن هذه الظواهر مع إرادتها من الكلام وثبوتها في نفس الأمر مثل للمعاد الروحاني واللذات والآلام العقلية، وكذا أكثر ظواهر القرآن على ما يذكره المحققون من علماء الإسلام لكان حقاً لا ريب فيه ولا اعتداد بمن ينميه .
And other such verses, and in the hadiths also many. In summary, the establishment of the Resurrection (al-hashr) is among the necessities of religion, and its denial is clear disbelief. If it is said: The verses indicating bodily resurrection are not more numerous or clearer than the verses indicating anthropomorphism (tashbīh), predestination (jabr), and decree (qadar), and similar things, and their interpretation (ta'wīl) has been obligated definitively, so let us also turn these to the explanation of spiritual resurrection, and the states of happiness and misery of souls after separation from bodies in a way that the common people understand; for the prophets are sent to all creatures to guide them to the path of truth, and to perfect their souls according to theoretical and practical power, and to preserve the order that leads to the goodness of all, and that is by encouragement and intimidation, by promise and threat, and by giving glad tidings of what they consider pleasure and perfection, and warning of what they consider pain and deficiency. Most of them are common people whose intellects fall short of understanding true perfections and intellectual pleasures, and are restricted to what they have encountered of sensory pleasures and pains, and have known of bodily perfections and deficiencies. So it is necessary that the prophets address them with what is an example (mithāl) of the true resurrection, as encouragement and intimidation for the common people, and to complete the matter of order. This is what Abū Naṣr al-Fārābī said: that religious speech is like examples and imaginings for philosophy. We say: Interpretation is only obligatory when the apparent meaning (ẓāhir) is impossible. And it is not impossible here, especially on the view that the body that is returned is like the first, not identical to it. And what you have mentioned of carrying the speech of the prophets and the texts of the Book to indicate an example of the resurrection of the soul and regard for the public interest is an accusation of the prophets of lying in what pertains to conveying (tablīgh) and intending to mislead the majority of creatures, and stubbornly spending one's life promoting falsehood and concealing truth, because they understand only these apparent meanings which, in your view, have no reality. Yes, if it were said: These apparent meanings, while being intended from the speech and established in reality, are like an example for spiritual resurrection and intellectual pleasures and pains, and similarly most apparent meanings of the Qur'an according to what the verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) among the scholars of Islam mention, it would be the truth without doubt, and no regard is given to those who deny it.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a defense of bodily resurrection against the claim that the verses should be interpreted metaphorically like verses of anthropomorphism. The author first states that affirming resurrection is a necessity of religion and its denial is kufr. Then he presents the objection: the verses on bodily resurrection are not more numerous or clear than verses that require interpretation (like tashbih), so they should also be interpreted as spiritual resurrection. The objector argues that prophets use such language to guide common people who only understand sensory things, so the real meaning is spiritual. This is the view of al-Farabi that religious narratives are examples for philosophy. The answer: interpretation is only needed when the apparent meaning is impossible. Here it is not impossible. Furthermore, to claim that prophets intentionally used false appearances to mislead the majority is to accuse them of lying. The correct view, according to the verifiers, is that these apparent meanings are true in themselves and also serve as examples for spiritual realities. So the denial is not to be heeded.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodily resurrection verses should be interpreted metaphorically because they are like anthropomorphism verses.
Correction: Al-Taftazani argues that interpretation is only needed when the literal meaning is impossible, which is not the case for bodily resurrection. Claiming otherwise accuses prophets of lying.

Al-Taftazani defends bodily resurrection by insisting on literal interpretation unless impossible and by rejecting the notion that prophets deceive.

First Objection: Recreating What Has Perished
Al-Taftazani presents the first objection of the deniers and its brief rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against bodily resurrection and the rebuttal that denies its two premises.

Core Idea
The first objection fails because its premises are simply denied.

Al-Taftazani begins the discussion of objections against bodily resurrection by presenting the first argument of the deniers. They claim that resurrection requires recreating what has completely perished, which they deem impossible. The rebuttal is swift: both premises of the objection are rejected outright. This lesson unpacks that objection and its counter.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: the objection (two premises) and the rebuttal.
The passage is short but dense; each word matters.
2
Identify the two premises
The objection has two premises: (1) that the body's mixture, life, composition, and forms have perished, and (2) that recreating them is impossible. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the rebuttal
Al-Taftazani says the objection is 'replied' by denying both premises. Reflect on what that means for the argument.
The denial is not elaborated here; it is a placeholder for later refutation.
4
Check your understanding
Take the checkpoint quiz to confirm you know the core point of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : احتج المنكرون بوجوه : الأول - (الأول) - انه مبني على إعادة المعدوم للقطع بفناء المزاج والحياة والتأليف والهيئات وقد ثبت استحالتها ورد بمنع المقدمتين .
He said: The deniers argued with several points: The first: That it (bodily resurrection) is based on recreating what has perished (i'ādat al-ma'dūm), because it is certain that the mixture (mizāj), life, composition (ta'līf), and forms (hay'āt) have perished, and their impossibility has been established. It was replied by denying the two premises.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection of those who deny bodily resurrection. The objection states that bodily resurrection is based on the concept of recreating what has ceased to exist (i'ādat al-ma'dūm). They argue that it is certain that the specific mixture (mizāj), life, composition (ta'līf), and forms (hay'āt) of the body have perished at death, and their impossibility of being recreated has been established. The rebuttal is very brief: the two premises (that these have perished and that it is impossible to recreate them) are simply denied. The author does not elaborate here, leaving the detailed response to later discussions.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم
المزاج
التأليف
الهيئات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodily resurrection requires recreating exactly the same material atoms that composed the body before death.
Correction: The objection assumes this, but the rebuttal denies that the original matter has perished completely or that recreation is impossible; later Islamic theology explains that Allah can recreate from a remnant (e.g., the coccyx bone) or create anew.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection against bodily resurrection mentioned in the text?
A) It requires the return of the soul to a new body
B) It requires recreating what has perished, which is impossible
C) It is not mentioned in the Quran
D) It contradicts reason

Key Takeaway
The first objection to bodily resurrection is based on the alleged impossibility of recreating what has perished, but it is dismissed by denying its two premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah can recreate you after death, even if your body has completely decayed? If yes, does that belief affect how you live today? What specifically will you do differently this week to prepare for that day?
?
The deniers argued from what they considered certain. Are there any 'certainties' in your life (like the impossibility of change, or the finality of death) that you need to re-examine in light of Allah's power? How would you practically remind yourself of this?
?
The rebuttal denies the premises. Are there doubts about the afterlife that you have accepted without questioning? What would it take for you to fully embrace the truth of bodily resurrection?

The Cannibalism Objection and the Reply of Original Parts
Al-Taftazani presents the cannibalism objection and the reply of original parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the cannibalism objection to bodily resurrection and how Al-Taftazani replies by distinguishing original parts from consumed parts.

Core Idea
Only the original parts of the body are resurrected, not the consumed parts.

One of the most famous objections to the resurrection of the body is the cannibalism problem: if a person eats another, how can both individuals be resurrected with their own bodies? Al-Taftazani presents this objection and then gives the Ash'ari response, which centers on the concept of 'original parts' (al-ajzā' al-aṣliyya). This lesson walks through the objection and its reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Notice the structure: an objection presented, then a reply.
The passage begins with 'الثاني' indicating this is the second objection.
2
Identify the three scenarios of the objection
The objection considers where eaten parts go: to the eater's body, to the eaten's body, or both. Each leads to a problem.
The text says 'إما أن تعاد في بدن الأكل... أو بالعكس... ولا سبيل إلى جعلها جزءاً من كل منهما'.
3
Understand the ethical problem
The objection raises an injustice: if a believer is eaten by a disbeliever, the obedient parts would be punished.
This is mentioned in 'تنعيم الأجزاء العاصية، وتعذيب المطيعة'.
4
Grasp the reply: original parts
The reply states that what is returned are the original parts, not the consumed parts. God preserves them from becoming original to another body.
Key phrase: 'ورد بأن المعاد هي الأجزاء الأصلية'.
5
Note the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila go further, saying this preservation is necessary for justice.
They say 'يجب ذلك ليصل الجزاء إلى مستحقه'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - لو أكل إنسان إنساناً فالأجزاء المأكولة إما أن تعاد في بدن الأكل، فلا يكون المأكول بعينه معاداً أو بالعكس، على أن لا أولوية، ولا سبيل إلى جعلها جزءاً من كل منهما، وأنه يلزم في أكل الكافر المؤمن تنعيم الأجزاء العاصية، وتعذيب المطيعة، ورد بأن المعاد هي الأجزاء الأصلية، فلا محذور، ولعل الله يحفظها من أن تصير جزءاً أصلياً لبدن آخر . بل عند المعتزلة يجب ذلك ليصل الجزاء إلى مستحقه . فإن قيل: مثل من يحيي العظام وهي رميم أئذا متنا وكنا تراباً يشعر بأن المتنازع إعادة الأجزاء بأسرها. قلنا لأنه ورد إزالة لاستبعادهم إحياء الرميم والتراب، والوارد لإثبات نفس الإعادة ايضاً كثير مثل : وهو الذي يبدأ الخلق ثم يعيده (۱) فسيقولون من يعيدنا قل الذي فطركم أول مرة ) ) (۲)
The second: If one human eats another human, then the eaten parts are either returned in the body of the eater, so the eaten person is not returned as the same individual, or the opposite, without any priority, and there is no way to make them a part of both; and it would necessitate, in the case of a believer eaten by a disbeliever, the reward of the disobedient parts and the punishment of the obedient parts. It was replied that what is returned are the original parts (al-ajzā' al-aṣliyya), so there is no difficulty, and perhaps God preserves them from becoming original parts of another body. In fact, according to the Mu'tazila, that (preservation) is necessary so that retribution reaches its due. If it is said: The example of 'Who revives bones while they are decayed' and 'When we have died and become dust' indicates that the matter in dispute is the return of all parts. We say: It came to remove their astonishment at the reviving of decayed bones and dust, and the verses that establish the very return are also many, such as 'And He it is who begins creation, then repeats it' and 'They will say: Who will return us? Say: He who first brought you into existence.'
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection of the deniers, known as the cannibalism problem. If a person eats another, how can the consumed parts be returned to both individuals? Either they go to the eater's body, then the eaten is not fully returned, or to the eaten's body, then the eater's body is incomplete. There is no way to assign them to both. Also, if a believer is eaten by a disbeliever, the obedient parts would be punished in the disbeliever's body and the disobedient parts rewarded in the believer's body, which is unjust. The answer: what is resurrected are the original parts (al-ajzā' al-aṣliyya) that constitute the individual's identity, not the consumed parts. God preserves those original parts from being incorporated as original parts of another body. The Mu'tazila even consider this preservation necessary for justice. A further objection is raised citing verses like 'Who revives bones while they are decayed?' which seems to imply the restoration of all parts. The reply: these verses are meant to remove their disbelief in the possibility of reviving decayed matter; they do not imply that every atom is restored. There are other explicit verses, such as 'He begins creation, then repeats it' and 'Who will return us? Say: He who first brought you into existence,' which clearly establish the return of the same individual.

Key Terms
الأجزاء الأصلية
إعادة
تنعيم
تعذيب
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that every atom of the body must return exactly as it was for resurrection to be valid.
Correction: The text clarifies that only the original parts (al-ajzā' al-aṣliyya) are returned; consumed parts do not need to be restored.

Checkpoint
According to the reply to the cannibalism objection, what parts of the body are resurrected?
A) All parts
B) The parts that were eaten
C) The original parts only
D) The soul only

Key Takeaway
The resurrection of the body requires only the return of the original parts that constitute personal identity, not all consumed parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only your 'original parts' will be restored, what does that mean for the way you treat your body today? Are you living as if your physical actions will be preserved and brought back?
?
The passage says that God preserves the original parts even if they are consumed by another. Does this truth change how you trust God's justice when you see evil seemingly unchallenged? How should it change your response to injustice?
?
Consider that the Mu'tazila argued preservation is necessary for justice. Do you live with the certainty that every part of your life—every deed and every thought—will be brought back for judgment? What is one habit you need to change because of this certainty?

The Scriptural Objection and the Distinction Between Possibility and Actuality
Al-Taftazani rebuts an objection from Quranic verses and provides supporting verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani responds to objections from Quranic verses that seem to imply the return of all parts.

Core Idea
Quranic verses about reviving bones prove possibility, not that every atom returns.

Some object that Quranic verses like 'Who revives bones while they are decayed' imply the return of all parts. Al-Taftazani replies that such verses are meant to show God's power over decayed matter, not to describe the exact mechanism. He then cites other verses that explicitly affirm the return of the same individual. This lesson examines this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the objection and the rebuttal.
The passage begins with 'فإن قيل' indicating an objection.
2
Identify the scriptural objection
The objector cites verses like 'Who revives bones while they are decayed' to argue that all parts must return.
The phrase 'يشعر بأن المتنازع إعادة الأجزاء بأسرها' shows the objector's conclusion.
3
Understand the reply: purpose of those verses
Al-Taftazani says those verses were revealed to remove astonishment at God's power, not to claim that every atom returns.
Key: 'قلنا لأنه ورد إزالة لاستبعادهم إحياء الرميم والتراب'.
4
Note the supporting verses
He then cites other verses that explicitly affirm the return itself, like 'He begins creation then repeats it'.
These verses directly support the doctrine of resurrection without specifying atom-by-atom return.

Third Objection on Resurrection: Purpose and Refutation
Al-Taftazani refutes the claim that resurrection must have a purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against bodily resurrection concerning purpose and al-Taftazani's threefold refutation.

Core Idea
Resurrection does not require a purpose in the way critics assume.

The third objection argues that resurrection must have a purpose, but possible purposes are flawed or imply deficiency. Al-Taftazani refutes this by denying the necessity of purpose, expanding the range of acceptable purposes, and rejecting the reduction of afterlife pleasure to mere relief from pain. This lesson unpacks the objection and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the trilemma of purpose
Read the first part of the passage that presents the three possibilities for resurrection: without purpose (futile), for God's benefit (deficiency), or for the servant's benefit (pain or pleasure).
Notice how the objector tries to force a flawed set of options.
2
Examine the three denials
Identify al-Taftazani's three refutations: denying that resurrection must have a purpose, denying that the mentioned purposes are the only possibilities, and denying that afterlife pleasure is merely removal of pain.
Each denial targets a different assumption in the objection.
3
Grasp the counter-proposal of retribution
Focus on the idea that delivering retribution to the one who deserves it is a valid purpose, independent of the objector's flawed alternatives.
This shows that the objection fails to consider all possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - أن الإعادة لا لغرض عبث ، ولغرض عائد إلى الله تعالى نقص، وإلى العبد إما إيصال ألم، وهو سفه، أو لذة، ولا لذة في الوجود، سيما في عالم الحس إذ هي خلاص عن ألم والإيلام ليعقبه الخلاص غير لائق بالحكمة . بمنع لزوم الغرض، ومنع انحصاره فيما ذكر، إذ ربما يكون إيصال الجزاء إلى المستحق غرضاً، ومنع كون اللذة الأخروية دفع الألم) .
The third: That the return (al-i'āda) is either without purpose, which is futile ('abath), or for a purpose returning to God, which is a deficiency (naqṣ), or for a purpose returning to the servant, which is either to cause pain, which is foolishness (safah), or pleasure, and there is no pleasure in existence, especially in the sensory world, since it is only relief from pain; and causing pain to be followed by relief is not fitting for wisdom. [It was replied] by denying the necessity of purpose, and denying its restriction to what was mentioned, for perhaps delivering retribution to the one who deserves it is a purpose, and denying that the afterlife pleasure is merely the removal of pain.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against bodily resurrection, concerning purpose. The objector argues that resurrection must have a purpose. If it has no purpose, it is futile (عبث). If the purpose returns to God, that implies a deficiency in God. If the purpose returns to the servant, it must be either to cause pain (which is foolish) or to give pleasure. But pleasure in the sensory world is only relief from pain, and causing pain only to later relieve it is not wise. The answer denies three things: first, the necessity that resurrection must have a purpose; second, the restriction of possible purposes to those mentioned, because delivering retribution to the deserving is a valid purpose; third, the claim that afterlife pleasure is merely the removal of pain. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
العبث
النقص
السفه
الجزاء
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Afterlife pleasure is merely the removal of pain.
Correction: Al-Taftazani denies this, asserting that afterlife pleasure is a positive experience, not just relief from pain.

Checkpoint
According to the third objection, if resurrection is for the servant's benefit, what two possibilities are considered?
A) Reward and punishment
B) Pleasure and pain
C) Sensible and intellectual
D) Good and evil

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it falsely assumes purpose is necessary and limits purposes to flawed options, whereas al-Taftazani shows that retribution is a valid purpose and afterlife pleasure is not merely pain relief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face trials or unanswered prayers, do you ever internally accuse Allah of acting without wisdom? How does this lesson's assertion that purpose is not necessary for divine acts confront your expectation that God must explain Himself to you?
?
If the ultimate retribution—justice from Allah—is a sufficient purpose for resurrection, what does that mean for how you view your own shortcomings? Are you truly living as if every deed will be repaid, or do you secretly hope that Allah will overlook your sins without consequence?
?
The objector claimed that causing pain for subsequent relief is not wise. Yet Allah allows pain in this world for eternal reward. Does this realization awaken in you a deeper trust in His wisdom, even when your present suffering seems pointless? Will you commit to surrendering your limited understanding to His perfect plan, starting today?

Dependence of Bodily Resurrection on Recreating the Non-Existent
Al-Taftazani states the rational impossibility of bodily resurrection due to dependence on recreating the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why bodily resurrection, if it requires recreating what has perished, is considered rationally impossible according to the kalam argument.

Core Idea
Bodily resurrection requires recreating the non-existent, which is impossible.

Al-Taftazani concludes his discussion by stating clearly why bodily resurrection poses a rational problem. The key is the concept of recreating something that has completely ceased to exist. He then explains two ways this could be thought, but both lead to the same conclusion of impossibility. Let us follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the author's phrasing.
Notice the use of 'إما ... وأما' which structures the two alternatives.
2
Identify the core premise
The first sentence states the premise: bodily resurrection depends on recreating the non-existent. Ask yourself why this is problematic.
3
Examine the two options
The author gives two ways to conceive resurrection: creation after annihilation or gathering after dispersal. Reflect on why both fail according to the text.
The failure of the second option is due to the perishing of composition, mixture, and life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن المعاد الجسماني موقوف على إعادة المعدوم وقد بان استحالتها وجـه التوقف إما على تقدير كونها إيجاداً بعد الفناء فظاهر . وأما على تقدير كونها جمعاً وإحياء بعد التفرق والموت فللقطع بفناء التأليف والمزاج والحياة، وكثير من الأغراض والهيئات.
Indeed, bodily resurrection depends on recreating what has perished, and its impossibility has become clear. The reason for the dependence is either on the assumption that it is creation after annihilation, which is obvious [in its impossibility], or on the assumption that it is gathering and reviving after dispersal and death, because it is certain that the composition, mixture, life, and many of the accidents (a'rāḍ) and forms (hay'āt) have perished.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion by restating the core difficulty: bodily resurrection requires recreating something that has ceased to exist (i'ādat al-ma'dūm), and this is impossible. The author then clarifies two ways this could be conceived. First, if resurrection is considered as a new creation after the original's annihilation, its impossibility is obvious. Second, if it is seen as gathering and reviving the dispersed parts after death, it is still impossible because the specific composition, mixture, life, and many accidents and forms have perished. This argument reinforces the first objection, emphasizing that regardless of how one conceptualizes the process, the obstacles remain insurmountable without divine intervention.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم
الإيجاد بعد الفناء
الجمع بعد التفرق
الإحياء بعد الموت
التأليف
المزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Gathering after dispersal avoids the problem of recreating from nothing.
Correction: The text points out that the original composition has perished, so even gathering requires recreating the non-existent.

Checkpoint
According to the concluding passage, what are the two possible ways of conceiving bodily resurrection?
A) As a new creation or as a renovation
B) As creation after annihilation or as gathering after dispersal
C) As a spiritual return or as a physical return
D) As a miracle or as a natural process

Key Takeaway
Bodily resurrection is rationally problematic because both creation after annihilation and gathering after dispersal require recreating what has ceased to exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quranic promises of resurrection, do you ever feel a silent tension between your rational mind that finds this concept difficult and your faith that accepts it? How do you reconcile this, or do you simply suppress the question?
?
If bodily resurrection were truly rationally impossible as argued here, would your hope of meeting Allah and the Prophet crumble? What would remain of your faith if this core belief were false?
?
Your body ages and changes every day. What makes you so certain that the exact same body—with its memories, scars, and identity—will be raised on the Last Day? What evidence do you cling to that overrides this logical objection?

The Third Objection: Purposelessness of Resurrection and Its Rebuttal
Argues that restoration without a purpose is futile, and that possible purposes are problematic; the rebuttal denies these claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against bodily resurrection—that it is purposeless—and how the rebuttal refutes it by denying the necessity of purpose, expanding possible purposes, and affirming positive afterlife pleasure.

Core Idea
Resurrection needs no purpose; if it did, recompense is a valid one.

This passage presents the third objection to bodily resurrection: that it appears purposeless and any possible purpose leads to absurdity. The objection argues that if restoration has no purpose, it is futile; if it has a purpose for God, that implies deficiency; if for the servant, it must be either painful (foolish) or pleasurable—but pleasure in this world is merely relief from pain, so inflicting pain for future relief is unwise. The rebuttal counters by denying that a purpose is necessary, denying that purposes are limited to those mentioned (delivering recompense is valid), and denying that afterlife pleasure is merely relief from pain; it is a positive pleasure. Through this, the rationality of bodily resurrection is defended.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's structure
The objection lists three problematic possibilities: no purpose, purpose for God, purpose for servant. Understand why each is deemed unacceptable.
The objection assumes purpose must be either for God or for the servant.
2
Explore the specific argument about pleasure
Notice that the objection claims pleasure is merely relief from pain, so inflicting pain for future relief is not wise. This is a key point.
The objection views pleasure as purely negative.
3
Learn the three rebuttal denials
The rebuttal denies: (1) that purpose is necessary, (2) that purposes are limited to those listed (recompense is another), (3) that afterlife pleasure is removal of pain.
The rebuttal offers a positive alternative.
4
Connect to the broader theology
Understand that this defense upholds the rationality of bodily resurrection, which is essential for Islamic eschatology.
This demonstrates that reason and revelation align.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - أن الإعادة لا لغرض عبث ، ولغرض عائد إلى الله تعالى نقص، وإلى العبد إما إيصال ألم، وهو سفه، أو لذة، ولا لذة في الوجود، سيما في عالم الحس إذ هي خلاص عن ألم والإيلام ليعقبه الخلاص غير لائق بالحكمة . بمنع لزوم الغرض، ومنع انحصاره فيما ذكر، إذ ربما يكون إيصال الجزاء إلى المستحق غرضاً، ومنع كون اللذة الأخروية دفع الألم).
Third - That restoration without a purpose is futile, and with a purpose that returns to God is a deficiency, and to the servant either the infliction of pain, which is foolish, or pleasure, and there is no pleasure in existence, especially in the sensory world, since it is relief from pain, and inflicting pain to be followed by relief is not befitting wisdom. It is rebutted by denying the necessity of a purpose, and denying its limitation to what was mentioned, for perhaps delivering recompense to its due is a purpose, and denying that the pleasure of the afterlife is the removal of pain.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against bodily resurrection and its rebuttal. The objection argues that restoration must have a purpose, otherwise it is futile. If the purpose pertains to God, it implies deficiency; if to the servant, it is either inflicting pain (which is foolish) or pleasure. But there is no true pleasure in existence, especially in the sensory world, because pleasure is merely the removal of pain. Inflicting pain for later relief is not befitting wisdom. The rebuttal denies three points: first, it is not necessary that restoration have a purpose; second, even if it does, the purpose is not limited to what the objection mentions—for example, delivering recompense to the deserving is a valid purpose; third, the assumption that the pleasure of the afterlife is relief from pain is denied (i.e., it is a real, positive pleasure). Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
الغرض (al-gharad)
العبث (al-'abath)
اللذة (al-ladhdha)
الألم (al-alam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pleasure is only the removal of pain, so there is no positive pleasure.
Correction: The rebuttal denies that afterlife pleasure is merely relief from pain; it is a real, positive pleasure.

Checkpoint
What is one of the purposes for resurrection that the rebuttal mentions as valid?
A) To demonstrate God's power.
B) Delivering recompense to the deserving.
C) To create new beings.
D) To test humans again.

Key Takeaway
The third objection fails because resurrection does not require a purpose, and even if it did, delivering just recompense is a valid purpose, and afterlife pleasure is genuinely positive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial that seems meaningless, do you secretly think God is being unjust or uncaring? How does the rebuttal's point that recompense is a valid purpose—even if you don't see it now—challenge that feeling?
?
The objection claims that inflicting pain for later pleasure is unwise. Have you ever experienced a period of intense difficulty that later brought you closer to God or taught you something valuable? Does that change how you view divine wisdom in your current hardships?
?
If resurrection does not need a purpose, yet God still chooses to recompense you, does your trust in His justice increase? Are you living today in a way that reflects confidence in that ultimate recompense, or are you seeking immediate relief from pain?

The First Objection: Returning the Non-Existent
First objection against bodily resurrection and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against bodily resurrection—that it requires returning the non-existent (i'adat al-ma'dum)—and how classical theologians rebutted it by denying the premises and redefining resurrection as restoration of parts.

Core Idea
Resurrection does not require bringing back nothing; it requires restoring the body's parts.

One of the most common philosophical objections to bodily resurrection is that it would require God to bring back something that has completely ceased to exist—an impossibility. In this passage, the author presents this objection and then shows how the theologian rebuts it by denying the impossibility and clarifying what resurrection actually entails. Let's examine this foundational debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is stated. Note the key claim: resurrection is based on 'return of the non-existent' (i'adat al-ma'dum) and that the body's components are annihilated.
The objection relies on two premises.
2
Understand the two premises being denied
The rebuttal denies both premises: (1) that the body is completely annihilated, and (2) that returning the non-existent is impossible. See how the author says 'rebutted by denying the two premises.'
This is a standard kalam technique.
3
Grasp the redefinition of resurrection
The author concedes for argument's sake but clarifies that resurrection means restoring parts to their previous composition and life, not returning the exact same entity. So the resurrected body is 'like' the beginning (mabda'), not identical.
This subtle shift avoids the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الأول) - انه مبني على إعادة المعدوم للقطع بفناء المزاج والحياة والتأليف والهيئات وقد ثبت استحالتها ورد بمنع المقدمتين . والجواب - منع امتناع الإعادة. وقد تكلمنا على أدلته ، ولوسلم، فالمراد إعادة الأجزاء إلى ما كانت عليه من التأليف والحياة، ونحو ذلك . ولا يضرنا كون المعاد مثل المبدأ، لاعينه.
(First) - It is based on the return of the non-existent, due to the certainty of the annihilation of temperament, life, composition, and states, and their impossibility has been established; it is rebutted by denying the two premises. The answer: denial of the impossibility of restoration. And we have already spoken about its proofs, and if it is conceded, then the meaning is the restoration of the parts to what they were of composition, life, and so forth. And it does not harm us that the restored (ma'ad) is like the beginning (mabda'), not the same as it.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against bodily resurrection and its rebuttal. The objection argues that bodily resurrection depends on the return of the non-existent (i'adat al-ma'dum), because temperament, life, composition, and states are known to perish. Since returning what has ceased to exist is impossible, resurrection is impossible. The rebuttal is simply to deny the two premises: the premise that the body is entirely annihilated and the premise that returning the non-existent is impossible. The book states that the impossibility of restoration is denied, and previous arguments have already addressed its proofs. Even if one concedes the impossibility, the intended meaning of resurrection is the restoration of the parts to their previous composition and life, not the return of the exact same entity in every respect. Therefore, it does not matter if the resurrected body is like the original rather than identical in all aspects.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم (i'adat al-ma'dum)
المعاد (al-ma'ad)
المبدأ (al-mabda')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the rebuttal accepts the impossibility of returning the non-existent.
Correction: The rebuttal denies the impossibility or, if conceded, redefines resurrection as restoration of parts, not return of the exact non-existent.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the first objection against bodily resurrection?
A) Resurrection requires the return of the non-existent, which is impossible.
B) The body is not annihilated so no return is needed.
C) The soul is immortal and does not need a body.
D) The Qur'an does not mention bodily resurrection.

Key Takeaway
The first objection to bodily resurrection is based on the impossibility of returning the non-existent, but theologians rebut it by denying the impossibility of restoration and clarifying that resurrection means restoring the body's parts to their previous state, not returning the exact same entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your resurrected body need not be numerically identical to your current body, does that weaken or strengthen your hope of being reunited with loved ones? How does this theological point affect the way you view your body today?
?
The objectors deemed resurrection impossible because they assumed it meant returning the exact same entity. But you are told it means restoration of parts. Are you living as if your current identity is tied to this specific material body, or are you preparing for a body that will be like it but better? What practical step can you take today to orient your heart toward that reality?
?
This passage defends resurrection through rational argument. But do you treat the resurrection as a mere intellectual doctrine to be defended, or as a truth that commands your entire life? If you truly believed that every atom of your body will be restored and judged, what would you change about your daily habits starting tomorrow?
