The Soul: Subtle Body and Afterlife Abode
Sanusi discusses the soul's nature as a subtle body and its abode after death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical Ash'ari view that the soul is a subtle body, and what happens to believers' and disbelievers' souls after death.

Core Idea
The soul is a subtle body that continues after death in a sensory abode.

Sanusi presents two interconnected teachings: where the souls of the righteous and wicked go after death, and what the soul actually is. The outward wording suggests souls are bodies, and he defends this by citing Imam al-Haramayn’s view that souls are subtle bodies intertwined with physical ones. This lesson unpacks both the doctrinal content and the rational framework behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping, then read the English translation. Notice that the passage moves from describing the afterlife states of souls to defining what the soul is.
The passage includes a citation to Imam al-Haramayn and references al-Irshad.
2
Identify the two abodes
The passage states that righteous souls are in 'crops of green birds in lanterns of light under the Throne' and later ascend to Paradise, while disbelievers' souls are lowered to Sijjin. Identify these two distinct fates.
The phrase 'حواصل طيور خضر' is a well-known hadith description.
3
Understand the soul's nature
The passage defines the soul as a subtle body (jism latif) and life (hayat) as an accident that subsists in it. Notice how this definition is used to explain the soul's separation from the body at death.
This reflects the Ash'ari position that the soul is not purely spiritual but has a bodily aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخصوصاً الصديقين والشهداء والصالحين، وأنها في حواصل طيور خضر في قناديل من نور معلقة تحت العرش، وإن كانت ظواهرها مشعرة بأن الأرواح من قبيل الأجسام على ما قال إمام الحرمين أن الأظهر عندنا أن الأرواح أجسام لطيفة مشابكة للأجسام المحسوسة، أجرى الله تعالى العادة باستمرار حياة الأجساد ما استمرت مشابكتها بها فإذا فارقها، يعقب الموت الحياة في إستمرار العادة، ثم · الروح يعرج به ، ويرفع في حواصل طيور خضر في الجنة، ويهبط به إلى سجين من الكفرة كما وردت فيه الآثار والحياة عرض يحيى به الجوهر . والروح يحيا بالحياة ايضاً إن قامت به الحياة، فهذا قولنا في الروح، كذا في الإرشاد.
And especially the truthful, the martyrs, and the righteous, and that they are in the crops of green birds in lanterns of light suspended beneath the Throne, even though their apparent meanings indicate that souls are of the category of bodies, according to what Imam al-Haramayn said: that the apparent to us is that souls are subtle bodies intertwined with sensible bodies. Allah, may He be exalted, has customary practice of continuing the life of bodies as long as their intertwining with them continues. When it separates from them, death follows life in the continuity of habit. Then the soul ascends with him, and is raised in the crops of green birds in Paradise, and is lowered to Sijjin (a place in Hell) for the disbelievers, as reported in the traditions. Life is an accident by which the substance is animated. And the soul also lives by life if life subsists in it. This is our saying concerning the soul. Thus in al-Irshad.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defines the soul (ruh) and its state after death. It begins by stating that the souls of the truthful, martyrs, and righteous are placed in the crops of green birds in lanterns of light under the Throne. Then it addresses a potential objection: the outward meaning suggests souls are bodies. It quotes Imam al-Haramayn, who asserts that the apparent view is that souls are subtle bodies (ajsam latifa) intertwined with physical bodies. Allah's custom is that bodies remain alive as long as the soul stays intertwined; when it separates, death follows. The passage then describes the soul's fate: the believer's soul ascends and resides in green birds in Paradise, while the disbeliever's soul is lowered to Sijjin. It defines life (hayat) as an accident that animates the substance, and confirms that the soul lives by that life if life subsists in it. The passage concludes by attributing this position to al-Irshad.

Key Terms
روح
أجسام لطيفة
حياة
عرض
حواصل
سجين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is a purely non-physical spirit with no bodily aspects.
Correction: Classical Sunni kalam, as represented by Sanusi and Imam al-Haramayn, holds that the soul is a subtle body, not a purely spiritual entity.

Checkpoint

A) It is placed in green birds in Paradise.
B) It is lowered to Sijjin.
C) It remains with the body.
D) It ascends to the Throne.

Key Takeaway
The soul is a subtle body whose post-mortem destination—whether green birds in Paradise or Sijjin—reflects its nature and the person's faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The truthful and martyrs are in green birds—are you striving to be among them? What sacrifice have you made today that proves your sincerity?

Preliminary Views on Permanence and the Threefold Division of Annihilation
Lists four views on permanence and introduces three categories of annihilation causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different theological positions on the nature of permanence (baqa') in bodies and recognize three general categories explaining how bodies undergo annihilation (fana').

Core Idea
Permanence is not a thing but a descriptive attribute, while annihilation has three possible causes.

Sanusi begins by reporting how various scholars define the permanence of bodies: some as a created accident, others as the accidents themselves, others as states (akwan), and al-Nazzam as continuous creation. He then reaffirms that bodies are permanent yet annihilable, and introduces the fundamental division of annihilation into three possible mechanisms: by direct removal, by opposite, or by removal of a condition. This sets the stage for the detailed arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the opening segment carefully to understand the different positions on permanence.
Note the key term 'baqa'' and its variants.
2
Identify the four positions on baqa'
List each scholar or group and their definition of permanence.
Al-Qadi has two opinions, but here he gives one.
3
Understand the status of bodies
The passage states bodies are permanent but not eternal, contrary to philosophers and some others.
This is a theological stance.
4
Observe the threefold classification
Notice the sentence introducing three theories: by removal of a remover, by occurrence of an opposite, or by removal of a condition.
This classification structures the entire debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصحابنا بقاء قائم بالجسم يخلق الله فيه حالاً فحالاً. وقال إمام الحرمين الأعراض التي تحت اتصاف الجسم بها. وقال القاضي في أحد قوليه : الأكوان التي يخلقها فيه حالاً فحالاً . وقال النظام : خلقه لأنه ليس بباق، بل يخلق حالاً فحالاً . قد سبقت في مباحث الجسم إشارة إلى أن الأجسام باقية، غير متزايلة على ما يراه النظام، وقابلة للفناء، غير دائمة للبقاء على ما يراه الفلاسفة قولاً بأنها أزلية أبدية . والجاحظ ، وجمع من الكرامية قولاً بأنها أبدية، غير أزلية. وتوقف أصحاب أبي الحسين في صحة الفناء واختلف القائلون بها في أن الفناء بإعدام معدم، أو بحدوث ضد، أو بانتفاء شرط. أما الأول - فذهب القاضي، وبعض المعتزلة إلى أن الله تعالى يعدم العالم بلا واسطة، فيصير معدوماً كما أوجده كذلك ، فصار موجوداً. وذهب أبو الهذيل إلى أنه تعالى يقول له : «افنَ، فيفنى كما قال له : كن فكان . وأما الثاني - فذهب جمهور المعتزلة إلى أن فناء الجوهر بحدوث ضد له الفناء، ثم اختلفوا فذهب ابن الأخشيد إلى أن الفناء، وإن لم يكن متحيزاً، لكنه يكون حاصلاً في جهة معينة . فإذا أحدثه الله تعالى فيها، عدمت الجواهر بأسرها. وذهب ابن شبيب إلى أن الله تعالى يحدث في كل جوهر فناء، ثم ذلك الفناء يقتضي عدم الجوهر في الزمان الثاني . وذهب أبو علي وأتباعه إلى أنه يخلق بعدد كل جوهر فناء لا في محل، فتفنى الجواهر. وقال أبو هاشم وأشياعه يخلق فناء واحد لا في محل، فتفنى به الجواهر بأسرها. وأما الثالث - وهو أن فناء الجوهر بانقطاع شرط وجوده . فزعم بشر أن ذلك الشرط بقاء يخلقه الله تعالى لا في محل، فإذا لم يوجد عدم الجوهر. وذهب الأكثرون من أصحابنا والكعبي من المعتزلة إلى أنه بقاء قائم به، يخلقه الله تعالى حالاً فحالاً. فإذا لم يخلقه الله تعالى فيه، انتفى الجوهر . وقال إمام الحرمين بأنها الأعراض التي يجب اتصاف الجسم بها. فإذا يخلقها الله فيه فني . وقال القاضي في أحد قوليه : هو الأكوان التي يخلقها الله تعالى في الجسم حالاً فحالاً. فمتى لم يخلقها فيه انعدم . وقال النظام إنه ليس بباق، بل يخلق حالاً فحالاً فمتى لم يخلق فني . وأكثر هذه الأقاويل من قبيل الأباطيل ، سيما القول: يكون الفناء أمراً محققاً في الخارج، وضداً للبقاء، قائماً بنفسه، أو بالجوهر، وكون البقاء موجوداً إلا في محل، ولعل وجه البطلان غني عن البيان .
Our companions [hold that] permanence (baqa') is an accident subsisting in the body, which God creates in it moment by moment. And Imam al-Haramayn [said]: the accidents (a'rad) by which the body is characterized. And al-Qadi in one of his two opinions [said]: the states (akwan) which He creates in it moment by moment. And al-Nazzam said: His creation [of it] because it is not permanent, rather He creates it moment by moment. There has already preceded, in the investigations of the body, an indication that bodies are permanent, not disintegrating according to al-Nazzam's view, and susceptible to annihilation, not eternal in permanence according to the philosophers' view, saying that they are eternal and everlasting. And al-Jahiz, and a group of the Karramiyya, saying that they are everlasting, not eternal. And the followers of Abu al-Husayn suspended judgment on the validity of annihilation. And those who affirmed it differed concerning whether annihilation is by the removal of a remover, or by the occurrence of an opposite, or by the removal of a condition. As for the first [view]: al-Qadi and some of the Mu'tazila went that God Most High annihilates the world without intermediary, so it becomes non-existent just as He brought it into existence in that manner, so it became existent. And Abu al-Hudhayl went that He Most High says to it: 'Perish!', so it perishes just as He said to it: 'Be!', so it was. As for the second [view]: the majority of the Mu'tazila went that the annihilation of the substance (jawhar) is by the occurrence of an opposite (didd) to it, namely annihilation. Then they differed: Ibn al-Akhshid went that annihilation, although not extended (mutahayyiz), occurs in a specific direction. So when God Most High produces it therein, all the substances become annihilated. And Ibn Shabib went that God Most High creates in every substance an annihilation, then that annihilation necessitates the non-existence of the substance in the second moment. And Abu 'Ali and his followers went that He creates for every substance an annihilation not in a locus (la fi mahall), so the substances become annihilated. And Abu Hashim and his followers said: He creates a single annihilation not in a locus, so by it all the substances become annihilated. As for the third [view]: that the annihilation of the substance is by the cessation of a condition of its existence. Bishr claimed that that condition is a permanence (baqa') which God Most High creates not in a locus, so if it does not exist, the substance becomes non-existent. And the majority of our companions and al-Ka'bi of the Mu'tazila went that it is a permanence subsisting in it, which God Most High creates in it moment by moment. So if God Most High does not create it in it, the substance ceases. And Imam al-Haramayn said that they are the accidents by which the body must be characterized. So if he creates them in it, it perishes. And al-Qadi in one of his two opinions [said]: they are the states which God Most High creates in the body moment by moment. So if He does not create them in it, it becomes non-existent. And al-Nazzam said: it is not permanent, rather He creates it moment by moment, so if He does not create it, it perishes. And most of these sayings are among falsehoods, especially the saying that annihilation is a thing realized externally, and an opposite to permanence, subsisting by itself or in the substance, and that permanence exists not in a locus. And perhaps the aspect of its falsehood is in no need of explanation.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques various theological opinions on the nature of the annihilation (fana') of bodies. It begins by summarizing earlier views on the nature of permanence (baqa'): some say it is an accident subsisting in the body (our companions), some say it is the accidents by which the body is characterized (Imam al-Haramayn), some say it is the states (akwan) (al-Qadi), and al-Nazzam says it is not permanent but created moment by moment. Then it notes that bodies are permanent and susceptible to annihilation, with differences on whether they are eternal or everlasting. The followers of Abu al-Husayn suspended judgment on annihilation, while those who affirmed it differed on its cause. The author then categorizes the opinions into three main views:

1. **First view: Direct annihilation by God.** Al-Qadi and some Mu'tazila hold that God annihilates the world directly without an intermediary. Abu al-Hudhayl says God commands it to perish, and it obeys.

2. **Second view: Annihilation by an opposite (didd).** The majority of Mu'tazila hold that annihilation occurs by the creation of an opposite to the substance, called 'annihilation' itself. Within this, there are four sub-views:
   - Ibn al-Akhshid: annihilation is a non-extended entity localized in a direction; when God creates it, all substances perish.
   - Ibn Shabib: God creates an annihilation in each substance, which then causes non-existence in the next moment.
   - Abu 'Ali and followers: God creates an annihilation for each substance, not in a locus, causing them to perish.
   - Abu Hashim and followers: God creates a single annihilation not in a locus, which annihilates all substances.

3. **Third view: Annihilation by removal of a condition of existence.** Bishr claims the condition is a permanence created by God not in a locus; if it ceases, the substance perishes. The majority of the author's companions and al-Ka'bi hold that permanence subsists in the body and is created moment by moment; if God stops creating it, the substance ceases. Imam al-Haramayn identifies the condition as the accidents necessary for the body; if God does not create them, the body perishes. Al-Qadi in one opinion says it is the states. Al-Nazzam says the body is not permanent but created moment by moment; if God does not create it, it perishes.

After enumerating these opinions, the author dismisses most as falsehoods (abateel), especially the claim that annihilation is a real external entity, an opposite to permanence, subsisting by itself or in the substance, and that permanence exists not in a locus. He says the falsehood is obvious and needs no explanation.

Key Terms
بقاء (baqa')
حالاً فحالاً (halan fa halan)
ضِدّ (didd)
شَرط (shart)
إعدام (i'dam)
مُعْدِم (mu'dim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which view says that permanence is the states (akwan) created moment by moment?
A) Companions
B) Imam al-Haramayn
C) Al-Qadi
D) Al-Nazzam

Key Takeaway
The debate on annihilation begins with understanding permanence, and three possible mechanisms are introduced.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence at this moment is being created by God anew, how would you live the next moment differently?
?
When you consider that annihilation can occur by the removal of a condition you rely on, what in your life are you treating as a permanent support that only God can sustain?
?
The passage shows scholars deeply pondering the nature of permanence. Have you ever stopped to realize that your own 'permanence' is an illusion? How does that truth shake the way you hold onto worldly things?

Unseen Matters
intermediate
8 min
Three Main Theories on the Cause of Annihilation (Fana')
Details the three theories of annihilation with sub-views for each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main theories on the cause of annihilation (fana') and the various sub-views within each, as debated among theologians.

Core Idea
Theologians proposed three causes for annihilation: direct divine action, creation of an opposite, or cessation of a condition.

In this passage, Sanusi expands on the three categories introduced earlier. For each, he reports the specific positions of various scholars, revealing a rich intra-tradition debate. The first theory holds that God directly annihilates; the second that an opposite called 'annihilation' is created; the third that a condition for existence is removed. Understanding these nuances is crucial for kalam ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the three main sections
The passage is divided into 'أما الأول' (first), 'وأما الثاني' (second), and 'وأما الثالث' (third). Identify each.
Each section contains multiple sub-views.
2
First theory: Direct removal by God
Al-Qadi says God annihilates without intermediary. Abu al-Hudhayl says God commands 'Perish!'.
Note the contrast: direct vs. command.
3
Second theory: Annihilation by opposite
Majority of Mu'tazila say an opposite called 'fana' is created. Sub-views differ on its locus and number.
Ibn al-Akhshid: localized; Ibn Shabib: in each substance; Abu Ali: each gets one; Abu Hashim: one for all.
4
Third theory: Cessation of a condition
Bishr says condition is a permanence not in a locus; companions say it's a permanence in the body; Imam al-Haramayn says it's the accidents; etc.
Nazzam: body is not permanent but continuously created.

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques various theological opinions on the nature of the annihilation (fana') of bodies. It begins by summarizing earlier views on the nature of permanence (baqa'): some say it is an accident subsisting in the body (our companions), some say it is the accidents by which the body is characterized (Imam al-Haramayn), some say it is the states (akwan) (al-Qadi), and al-Nazzam says it is not permanent but created moment by moment. 1. **First view: Direct annihilation by God.** Al-Qadi and some Mu'tazila hold that God annihilates the world directly without an intermediary. Abu al-Hudhayl says God commands it to perish, and it obeys.

2. **Second view: Annihilation by an opposite (didd).** The majority of Mu'tazila hold that annihilation occurs by the creation of an opposite to the substance, called 'annihilation' itself. Within this, there are four sub-views:

   - Abu 'Ali and followers: God creates an annihilation for each substance, not in a locus, causing them to perish.
   - Abu Hashim and followers: God creates a single annihilation not in a locus, which annihilates all substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading this detailed debate on annihilation, do you feel a greater awe for God's power, or does the complexity trouble you? How does seeing theologians wrestle with these questions affect your own trust in divine wisdom?
?
If the body is sustained moment by moment (as some Ash'aris held), then your very existence is dependent on God's continuous act of preservation. How should this awareness change the way you begin each moment of your day? What specific habit can you adopt to remind yourself of this constant dependence?
?
The theologians were not satisfied with a vague belief in the afterlife; they sought to understand the mechanism of annihilation. Are you willing to invest similar intellectual effort into understanding your faith's doctrines, or do you rely on general slogans? What one theological question will you commit to studying more deeply this week?

Unseen Matters

Critique of False Opinions on Annihilation
Critiques the majority of opinions on annihilation as falsehoods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's dismissal of most opinions on annihilation, especially the claim that annihilation is a real entity opposed to permanence.

Core Idea
Sanusi condemns the view that annihilation is a real external thing as an obvious falsehood.

After enumerating the many positions on annihilation, Sanusi delivers a sharp critique. He declares that most of these sayings are falsehoods (abateel), particularly the claim that annihilation is a real entity existing externally, that it is an opposite to permanence, or that permanence exists not in a locus. He says its falsehood requires no explanation. This lesson shows the author's own stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the final sentence
Read the Arabic sentence carefully: 'وأكثر هذه الأقاويل من قبيل الأباطيل...'
Note the strong language.
2
Identify the specifically rejected claims
Sanusi singles out the claim that fana' is a thing realized externally and an opposite to baqa', subsisting by itself or in the substance, and that baqa' exists not in a locus.
These are the metaphysical positions he finds untenable.
3
Reflect on the reason for rejection
Sanusi says 'the aspect of its falsehood is in no need of explanation'. Consider why he thinks it is self-evidently wrong.
It might contradict the basic Kalam understanding that accidents do not subsist by themselves.

أصحابنا بقاء قائم بالجسم يخلق الله فيه حالاً فحالاً. وقال إمام الحرمين الأعراض التي تحت اتصاف الجسم بها. وقال القاضي في أحد قوليه : الأكوان التي يخلقها فيه حالاً فحالاً . وقال النظام : خلقه لأنه ليس بباق، بل يخلق حالاً فحالاً . قد سبقت في مباحث الجسم إشارة إلى أن الأجسام باقية، غير متزايلة على ما يراه النظام، وقابلة للفناء، غير دائمة للبقاء على ما يراه الفلاسفة قولاً بأنها أزلية أبدية . والجاحظ ، وجمع من الكرامية قولاً بأنها أبدية، غير أزلية. وتوقف أصحاب أبي الحسين في صحة الفناء واختلف القائلون بها في أن الفناء بإعدام معدم، أو بحدوث ضد، أو بانتفاء شرط. أما الأول - فذهب القاضي، وبعض المعتزلة إلى أن الله تعالى يعدم العالم بلا واسطة، فيصير معدوماً كما أوجده كذلك ، فصار موجوداً. وذهب أبو الهذيل إلى أنه تعالى يقول له : «افنَ، فيفنى كما قال له : كن فكان . وأما الثاني - فذهب جمهور المعتزلة إلى أن فناء الجوهر بحدوث ضد له الفناء، ثم اختلفوا فذهب ابن الأخشيد إلى أن الفناء، وإن لم يكن متحيزاً، لكنه يكون حاصلاً في جهة معينة . فإذا أحدثه الله تعالى فيها، عدمت الجواهر بأسرها. وذهب ابن شبيب إلى أن الله تعالى يحدث في كل جوهر فناء، ثم ذلك الفناء يقتضي عدم الجوهر في الزمان الثاني . وذهب أبو علي وأتباعه إلى أنه يخلق بعدد كل جوهر فناء لا في محل، فتفنى الجواهر. وقال أبو هاشم وأشياعه يخلق فناء واحد لا في محل، فتفنى به الجواهر بأسرها. وأما الثالث - وهو أن فناء الجوهر بانقطاع شرط وجوده . فزعم بشر أن ذلك الشرط بقاء يخلقه الله تعالى لا في محل، فإذا لم يوجد عدم الجوهر. وذهب الأكثرون من أصحابنا والكعبي من المعتزلة إلى أنه بقاء قائم به، يخلقه الله تعالى حالاً فحالاً. فإذا لم يخلقه الله تعالى فيه، انتفى الجوهر . وقال إمام الحرمين بأنها الأعراض التي يجب اتصاف الجسم بها. فإذا يخلقها الله فيه فني . وقال القاضي في أحد قوليه : هو الأكوان التي يخلقها الله تعالى في الجسم حالاً فحالاً. فمتى لم يخلقها فيه انعدم . وقال النظام إنه ليس بباق، بل يخلق حالاً فحالاً فمتى لم يخلق فني . وأكثر هذه الأقاويل من قبيل الأباطيل ، سيما القول: يكون الفناء أمراً محققاً في الخارج، وضداً للبقاء، قائماً بنفسه، أو بالجوهر، وكون البقاء موجوداً إلا في محل، ولعل وجه البطلان غني عن البيان .
Our companions [hold that] permanence (baqa') is an accident subsisting in the body, which God creates in it moment by moment. And Imam al-Haramayn [said]: the accidents (a'rad) by which the body is characterized. And al-Qadi in one of his two opinions [said]: the states (akwan) which He creates in it moment by moment. And al-Nazzam said: His creation [of it] because it is not permanent, rather He creates it moment by moment. There has already preceded, in the investigations of the body, an indication that bodies are permanent, not disintegrating according to al-Nazzam's view, and susceptible to annihilation, not eternal in permanence according to the philosophers' view, saying that they are eternal and everlasting. And al-Jahiz, and a group of the Karramiyya, saying that they are everlasting, not eternal. And the followers of Abu al-Husayn suspended judgment on the validity of annihilation. And those who affirmed it differed concerning whether annihilation is by the removal of a remover, or by the occurrence of an opposite, or by the removal of a condition. As for the first [view]: al-Qadi and some of the Mu'tazila went that God Most High annihilates the world without intermediary, so it becomes non-existent just as He brought it into existence in that manner, so it became existent. And Abu al-Hudhayl went that He Most High says to it: 'Perish!', so it perishes just as He said to it: 'Be!', so it was. As for the second [view]: the majority of the Mu'tazila went that the annihilation of the substance (jawhar) is by the occurrence of an opposite (didd) to it, namely annihilation. Then they differed: Ibn al-Akhshid went that annihilation, although not extended (mutahayyiz), occurs in a specific direction. So when God Most High produces it therein, all the substances become annihilated. And Ibn Shabib went that God Most High creates in every substance an annihilation, then that annihilation necessitates the non-existence of the substance in the second moment. And Abu 'Ali and his followers went that He creates for every substance an annihilation not in a locus (la fi mahall), so the substances become annihilated. And Abu Hashim and his followers said: He creates a single annihilation not in a locus, so by it all the substances become annihilated. As for the third [view]: that the annihilation of the substance is by the cessation of a condition of its existence. Bishr claimed that that condition is a permanence (baqa') which God Most High creates not in a locus, so if it does not exist, the substance becomes non-existent. And the majority of our companions and al-Ka'bi of the Mu'tazila went that it is a permanence subsisting in it, which God Most High creates in it moment by moment. So if God Most High does not create it in it, the substance ceases. And Imam al-Haramayn said that they are the accidents by which the body must be characterized. So if he creates them in it, it perishes. And al-Qadi in one of his two opinions [said]: they are the states which God Most High creates in the body moment by moment. So if He does not create them in it, it becomes non-existent. And al-Nazzam said: it is not permanent, rather He creates it moment by moment, so if He does not create it, it perishes. And most of these sayings are among falsehoods, especially the saying that annihilation is a thing realized externally, and an opposite to permanence, subsisting by itself or in the substance, and that permanence exists not in a locus. And perhaps the aspect of its falsehood is in no need of explanation.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques various theological opinions on the nature of the annihilation (fana') of bodies. It begins by summarizing earlier views on the nature of permanence (baqa'): some say it is an accident subsisting in the body (our companions), some say it is the accidents by which the body is characterized (Imam al-Haramayn), some say it is the states (akwan) (al-Qadi), and al-Nazzam says it is not permanent but created moment by moment. Then it notes that bodies are permanent and susceptible to annihilation, with differences on whether they are eternal or everlasting. The followers of Abu al-Husayn suspended judgment on annihilation, while those who affirmed it differed on its cause. The author then categorizes the opinions into three main views:

1. **First view: Direct annihilation by God.** Al-Qadi and some Mu'tazila hold that God annihilates the world directly without an intermediary. Abu al-Hudhayl says God commands it to perish, and it obeys.

2. **Second view: Annihilation by an opposite (didd).** The majority of Mu'tazila hold that annihilation occurs by the creation of an opposite to the substance, called 'annihilation' itself. Within this, there are four sub-views:
   - Ibn al-Akhshid: annihilation is a non-extended entity localized in a direction; when God creates it, all substances perish.
   - Ibn Shabib: God creates an annihilation in each substance, which then causes non-existence in the next moment.
   - Abu 'Ali and followers: God creates an annihilation for each substance, not in a locus, causing them to perish.
   - Abu Hashim and followers: God creates a single annihilation not in a locus, which annihilates all substances.

3. **Third view: Annihilation by removal of a condition of existence.** Bishr claims the condition is a permanence created by God not in a locus; if it ceases, the substance perishes. The majority of the author's companions and al-Ka'bi hold that permanence subsists in the body and is created moment by moment; if God stops creating it, the substance ceases. Imam al-Haramayn identifies the condition as the accidents necessary for the body; if God does not create them, the body perishes. Al-Qadi in one opinion says it is the states. Al-Nazzam says the body is not permanent but created moment by moment; if God does not create it, it perishes.

After enumerating these opinions, the author dismisses most as falsehoods (abateel), especially the claim that annihilation is a real external entity, an opposite to permanence, subsisting by itself or in the substance, and that permanence exists not in a locus. He says the falsehood is obvious and needs no explanation.

Key Terms
أباطيل (abateel)
محقق (muhaqqaq)
ضد (didd)
قائم بنفسه (qa'im bi nafsihi)
لا في محل (la fi mahall)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Annihilation is a real, independently existing entity that opposes permanence.
Correction: Sanusi declares this a falsehood; in Kalam, attributes cannot exist independently and annihilation is not a thing.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi say about most of the opinions on annihilation?
A) They are correct.
B) They require further study.
C) They are falsehoods.
D) They are likely true.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi dismisses the notion of annihilation as an independent reality as an obvious falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fear losing something precious, do you treat that loss as a real 'entity' that exists against you? How does Sanusi's dismissal of annihilation as an external falsehood challenge you to rethink your fears as illusions rather than realities?
?
Sanusi says the falsehood is 'in no need of explanation'. Are there false beliefs in your own life that you have accepted without question—perhaps about your own worth or the permanence of worldly things—that are equally obvious once you examine them? What would it take to expose them?
?
If you truly believed that annihilation is not a real thing, how would your attachment to this life change? Would you still cling to people, possessions, or status, or would you invest more in what is truly permanent?

The Nature of Resurrection: Suspension of Judgment
Sanusi presents two views on resurrection and advocates tawaqquf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether resurrection is creation after annihilation or gathering of scattered parts, and why the author advocates suspension of judgment.

Core Idea
The true nature of resurrection is rationally inconclusive; one must suspend judgment.

Sanusi introduces the fourth investigation of the sam'iyyat, concerning the nature of the resurrection (al-hashr). Two competing positions are presented: resurrection as a new creation after annihilation, or as a reassembly of scattered parts. Rather than siding with either, Sanusi declares the correct stance to be suspension of judgment (tawaqquf), and then reports—and counters—two proofs for the first view. This lesson unpacks the debate and the reasoning behind tawaqquf.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise style of reporting views and proofs.
2
Identify the two positions and the author's verdict
Find where Sanusi states the two views on resurrection and his own position. Note the phrase 'wa-al-haqq al-tawaqquf' (the truth is suspension).
This is the key claim of the passage.
3
Examine the two proofs for the first view
The passage lists two proofs: consensus (ijma') and a Quranic verse. Notice how each is presented and how the first is rebutted.
The second proof is not rebutted explicitly, implying it also falls short.
4
Reflect on why the author suspends judgment
Consider why neither proof is conclusive. The first is denied; the second may have counter-arguments. This leads to tawaqquf.
Tawaqquf is a legitimate theological stance when evidence is balanced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الرابع - واختلفوا في أن الحشر إيجاد بعد الفناء، أو جمع التفرق، والحق التوقف. احتج الأولون بوجوه : الأول - الإجماع قبل ظهور المخالفين ورد بالمنع . الثاني - قوله تعالى : هو الأول والآخر . ولا يتصور إلا بانعدام المخلوقات وليس بعد القيمة ،وفاقاً، فيكون قبلها.
He said: The fourth investigation – And they differed concerning whether the resurrection (al-hashr) is creation after annihilation, or a gathering of what is scattered, and the truth is suspension (al-tawaqquf). The proponents of the first [view] argued with proofs: the first – consensus before the appearance of opponents, and it was rebutted by denial. The second – His statement Most High: 'He is the First and the Last' (57:3). And it cannot be conceived except by the annihilation of created beings, and it is not after the Hereafter, by agreement, so it would be before it.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth investigation (mabhas rabi') concerning the nature of resurrection (al-hashr). Two opposing views are stated: (1) resurrection is creation (by God) after annihilation of the world, and (2) resurrection is a gathering together of what has become scattered (i.e., the physical parts of bodies). The author declares that the correct position is suspension of judgment (tawaqquf). He then reports two proofs given by proponents of the first view (creation after annihilation):

- First proof: consensus (ijma') before the appearance of opponents. This is rejected by countering that such consensus cannot be established (i.e., it is not universally agreed upon).
- Second proof: the Qur'anic verse 'He is the First and the Last' (Surah al-Hadid 57:3). The proponents argue that for God to be the Last, all created beings must first be annihilated, and that this annihilation occurs before the Hereafter (al-qiyama), not after it, so there is creation after that annihilation. The author does not explicitly reject this proof here but implies by his earlier statement that the truth is tawaqquf that the evidence is inconclusive.

Key Terms
الحشر
إيجاد بعد الفناء
جمع التفرق
التوقف
الإجماع
الآخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must definitively choose either that resurrection is recreation after annihilation or that it is gathering of parts.
Correction: The author argues that the evidence is inconclusive, so the correct stance is to suspend judgment (tawaqquf).

Checkpoint
What is the author's position on whether resurrection is creation after annihilation or gathering of scattered parts?
A) Creation after annihilation
B) Gathering of scattered parts
C) Suspension of judgment
D) Both are true

Key Takeaway
The resurrection's nature is not rationally decisive; one should suspend judgment between recreation and reassembly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a disagreement among scholars about a matter of faith, do you tend to side with the majority without thorough investigation? How does Sanusi's example of tawaqquf challenge your willingness to admit ignorance?
?
The author argues that the true nature of resurrection is not rationally conclusive. Does this unsettle you, or does it deepen your reliance on Allah's revelation? Which parts of your belief rest on shaky proofs?
?
Are there theological questions you've been avoiding because they are uncomfortable? What would it mean for you to suspend judgment on something you've always assumed, and live with that uncertainty before Allah?

Consensus as Proof for Resurrection and Its Refutation
Presents the consensus argument and its refutation by denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus argument for resurrection after annihilation and how it is refuted by simply denying that consensus existed.

Core Idea
Consensus is only valid if it truly exists; denial refutes the claim.

In this lesson, we encounter the first proof presented by proponents of resurrection as creation after annihilation: the claim of early Muslim consensus. The author states this argument and then immediately refutes it by rejecting the premise that there ever was such a consensus. This concise exchange introduces a key method of theological critique—challenging the factual basis of a proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its English translation. Note the structure: first the claim (consensus before opponents), then the refutation (denial).
The passage is very concise; every word matters.
2
Understand the consensus argument
Those who believe in resurrection after annihilation argue that early Muslims agreed on this, and no dissenting voice appeared until later. This would make the belief authoritative.
Consensus is a strong proof in Islamic theology.
3
Analyze the refutation
The refutation is simply 'denial'—meaning the claim of consensus is rejected. This could be because later disagreement shows consensus never formed, or because the claim is unverifiable.
Notice that the author does not offer a lengthy argument; the denial itself suffices.
4
Reflect on the method
Ask yourself: Why is denying the existence of consensus a valid refutation? Because if the consensus never existed, the proof collapses.
This shows the importance of verifying the premises of an argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول - الإجماع قبل ظهور المخالفين ورد بالمنع.
First - consensus before the appearance of opponents, and it was refuted by denial.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This is the first proof presented by those who argue for resurrection as creation after annihilation (al-awwalūn). They claim that there was a consensus (ijmāʿ) among early Muslims that resurrection occurs after annihilation, before any dissenting view appeared. The refutation (jawāb) given here is simply that this consensus is denied (mumtaniʿ) – i.e., the claim of consensus is rejected. The author does not elaborate further, but the point is that the existence of later disagreement shows that consensus never actually held, or that the claim is unsubstantiated.

Key Terms
الإجماع (consensus)
المنع (denial)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That consensus is always a valid proof in theological arguments.
Correction: The consensus argument is only valid if the consensus actually existed. Here, the refutation denies its existence, showing that the mere claim of consensus is not sufficient.

Checkpoint
How is the consensus argument for resurrection after annihilation refuted?
A) By saying the consensus was later abrogated
B) By denying the consensus ever existed
C) By claiming consensus is not a valid source in kalām
D) By saying the consensus only applied to the first generation

Key Takeaway
The consensus argument for resurrection is refuted by denying that the consensus ever existed, showing that no proof can stand on an unsubstantiated premise.

Resurrection: Disagreement and the Position of Tawaqquf
Disagreement on resurrection and affirmation of tawaqquf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement among Muslim scholars on the nature of resurrection and why the author chooses to suspend judgment (tawaqquf).

Core Idea
Resurrection's mode is known only to Allah; we suspend judgment.

This lesson explores the fourth investigation (al-mabḥath al-rābiʿ) on resurrection. Scholars differed: is resurrection creation after annihilation or reassembly of scattered parts? The author presents both views, examines key scriptural proofs, and then affirms the position of tawaqquf (suspension) as the truth, following Imam al-Haramayn.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the author states the disagreement first, then lists proofs and refutations.
2
Identify the two views
The passage presents two views on resurrection: creation after annihilation (ījād baʿd al-fanāʾ) and collection of scattered parts (jamʿ al-tafarruq).
3
Examine the five proofs and refutations
The first group brings five scriptural proofs. For each, note the proof and the refutation given. This helps understand why the author chooses tawaqquf.
The proofs include verses like 'al-Awwal wa al-Akhir', 'kullu shay'in hālik', etc.
4
Understand tawaqquf
After listing the arguments, the author declares that the truth is tawaqquf (suspension). He quotes Imam al-Haramayn explaining that both possibilities are intellectually valid and no decisive textual proof specifies one.
Tawaqquf is not uncertainty; it is recognizing the limits of human knowledge.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why the author might have chosen tawaqquf over either view. How does this approach respect both reason and revelation?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: المبحث الرابع - واختلفوا في أن الحشر إيجاد بعد الفناء، أو جمع التفرق، والحق التوقف. احتج الأولون بوجوه: الأول - الإجماع قبل ظهور المخالفين ورد بالمنع. الثاني - قوله تعالى: {هو الأول والآخر} ولا يتصور إلا بانعدام المخلوقات وليس بعد القيمة، وفاقاً، فيكون قبلها. وأجيب بأن المعنى هو المبدأ أو الغاية، أو هو الآلة لا غير، أو هو الباقي بعد موت الأحياء، أو هو الأول خلقاً، والآخر رزقاً. الثالث - قوله تعالى: {كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه} وليس المراد الخروج عن الانتفاع، لأن منفعة الدلالة على الصانع باقية بعد التفرق. وأجيب بأن الإمكان هلاك في نفسه، وكذا الخروج عن الانتفاع الذي خلق الشيء لأجله، وإن صلح لمنفعة أخرى، وليس خلق كل جوهر للاستدلال. الرابع - قوله تعالى: {وهو الذي يبدأ الخلق ثم يعيده} {كما بدأنا أول خلق نعيده} والبدء من العدم، فكذا العود. وأجيب بأن بدء الخلق قد لا يكون عن عدم، قال الله تعالى: {وبدأ خلق الإنسان من طين}. الخامس - قوله تعالى: {كل من عليها فان} وأجيب بأن الفناء قد يكون بالخروج عن الانتفاع المقصود مثل: فني الزاد والطعام، وأفناهم الحرب. يعني أن القائلين بصحة الفناء، وبحقية حشر الأجساد اختلفوا في أن ذلك بإيجاد بعد الفناء، أو بالجمع بعد تفرق الأجزاء، والحق التوقف، وهو اختيار إمام الحرمين، حيث قال: يجوز عقلاً أن تعدم الجواهر، ثم تعاد، وأن تبقى وتزول أعراضها المعهودة ثم تعاد بنيتها، ولم يدل قاطع سمعي على تعيين أحدهما. فلا يبعد أن يغير أجسام العباد على صفة أجسام التراب، ثم يعاد تركيبها إلى ما عهد، ولا نحيل أن يعدم منها شيء، ثم يعاد، والله أعلم.
He said: The fourth investigation - They differed as to whether resurrection is creation after annihilation or collection of scattered parts, and the truth is suspension (tawaqquf). The first group argued with proofs: First - consensus before the appearance of opponents, and it was refuted by denial. Second - His statement, the Exalted: 'He is the First and the Last' - and it is not conceivable except by the annihilation of creatures, and there is nothing after the Hereafter, by agreement, so it is before it. And it was answered that the meaning is the Beginning or the End, or He is the Instrument and nothing else, or He is the Abiding after the death of living things, or He is the First in creation and the Last in provision. Third - His statement, the Exalted: 'Everything perishes except His Face' - and the intended meaning is not removal from benefit, because the benefit of indicating the Maker remains after scattering. And it was answered that possibility is perishing in itself, and likewise removal from the benefit for which the thing was created, even if it is suitable for another benefit, and not every substance is created for indication. Fourth - His statement, the Exalted: 'It is He who begins creation then returns it' and 'As We began the first creation, We will return it' - and the beginning is from non-existence, so similarly the return. And it was answered that the beginning of creation may not be from non-existence, God said, the Exalted: 'And He began the creation of man from clay.' Fifth - His statement, the Exalted: 'Everyone upon it will perish' - and it was answered that perishing may be by removal from the intended benefit, like 'the provision and food perished' and 'the war destroyed them.' Meaning that those who affirm the validity of annihilation and the reality of the resurrection of bodies differed on whether it is by creation after annihilation or by collection after the scattering of parts, and the truth is suspension, and it is the choice of Imam al-Haramayn, for he said: It is intellectually permissible that substances are annihilated then recreated, and that they remain while their customary accidents cease then their structure is restored, and no decisive scriptural proof has come determining one of the two. So it is not far that the bodies of servants are changed to the form of dirt bodies, then their composition is restored to what was customary, and we do not consider it impossible that something of them is annihilated then restored, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the fourth investigation (al-mabḥath al-rābiʿ) concerning resurrection (al-ḥashr). The author states the disagreement: Is resurrection the creation of bodies anew after annihilation (ījād baʿd al-fanāʾ) or the collection of scattered parts after disintegration (jamʿ al-tafarruq)? He then declares that the correct position is to withhold judgment (al-ḥaqq al-tawaqquf). He then provides the arguments of the first group (those who affirm annihilation and recreation) along with their refutations. These include: (1) consensus before the appearance of dissenters, refuted by denial; (2) the verse 'He is the First and the Last' interpreted as beginning and end or other meanings; (3) the verse 'Everything perishes except His Face' answered by noting that possibility itself perishes or that benefit is lost; (4) the verse about beginning and returning creation, answered by noting that creation can begin from something as from clay; (5) the verse 'Everyone upon it will perish' answered by interpreting perishing as losing intended benefit. After listing these, the author clarifies that the disagreement among those who accept both annihilation and bodily resurrection is over the mode: whether it occurs through recreation after annihilation or through reassembly of scattered parts. He reaffirms tawaqquf as the truth, citing Imam al-Haramayn, who states that both are intellectually possible and no decisive scriptural proof specifies one. He adds that it is not far-fetched that bodies become like dust and then are recomposed. The lesson concludes with 'God knows best,' signaling the author's deference to divine knowledge.

Key Terms
حشر (resurrection)
إيجاد بعد الفناء (creation after annihilation)
جمع التفرق (collection of scattered parts)
توقف (suspension)
إمام الحرمين (Imam al-Haramayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That resurrection must necessarily be one of the two modes (creation after annihilation or collection of parts) and that one must pick a side.
Correction: The author teaches that both modes are intellectually possible, and the correct position is to suspend judgment (tawaqquf) because no decisive scriptural proof specifies one.

Checkpoint
What is the author's stated position on the nature of resurrection?
A) It is definitely creation after annihilation
B) It is definitely collection of scattered parts
C) The truth is to withhold judgment (tawaqquf)
D) It is a spiritual resurrection only

Key Takeaway
When scriptural and rational arguments do not decisively favor one view over another, the proper stance is tawaqquf (suspension of judgment), as affirmed by Imam al-Haramayn regarding the mode of resurrection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a matter of faith that has no clear answer, do you feel pressured to commit to a position, or can you sincerely say 'God knows best' and leave it at that? Where in your life right now might you need to practice tawaqquf instead of forcing certainty?
?
The passage shows that even strong-sounding scriptural arguments can be refuted. Are there beliefs you hold that you have never questioned because they seemed obvious? Would you be willing to suspend judgment if you realized your evidence was not decisive?
?
Imam al-Haramayn said both modes of resurrection are intellectually possible, yet he did not reject the reality of resurrection itself. Is there a truth you affirm wholeheartedly—like the resurrection—while remaining humble about its details? Ask yourself: Are you trusting God’s knowledge more than your own reasoning?

Refuting the 'First and Last' Proof
The verse as proof for annihilation and its alternative interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'He is the First and the Last' does not prove the annihilation of all creatures, and you will learn the alternative interpretations that maintain the verse's flexibility.

Core Idea
The verse 'He is the First and the Last' does not require annihilation; it has multiple valid meanings.

This lesson examines the second proof presented by those who claim that God will annihilate all creation. They argue that the verse 'He is the First and the Last' implies that God is alone after everything else, requiring annihilation. However, Muslim scholars have offered several alternative interpretations that show the verse does not necessitate universal annihilation. We will explore these interpretations and understand the flexibility of Qur'anic exegesis.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how the proof is presented and then refuted with multiple alternative meanings.
2
Understand the proof's reasoning
Identify why the proof claims that 'He is the First and the Last' entails annihilation. It argues that God being 'Last' requires that creatures cease to exist before the Hereafter.
3
Examine the five interpretations
List the five interpretations given in the refutation: Beginning/End, Instrument, Abiding after death, First in creation and Last in provision. Understand how each avoids the annihilation conclusion.
4
Reflect on exegetical flexibility
Consider that the verse can bear multiple valid meanings, showing that it is not a decisive proof for annihilation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - قوله تعالى: {هو الأول والآخر} ولا يتصور إلا بانعدام المخلوقات وليس بعد القيمة، وفاقاً، فيكون قبلها. وأجيب بأن المعنى هو المبدأ أو الغاية، أو هو الآلة لا غير، أو هو الباقي بعد موت الأحياء، أو هو الأول خلقاً، والآخر رزقاً.
Second - His statement, the Exalted: 'He is the First and the Last' and it is not conceivable except by the annihilation of creatures, and there is nothing after the Hereafter, by agreement, so it is before it. And it was answered that the meaning is the Beginning or the End, or He is the Instrument and nothing else, or He is the Abiding after the death of living things, or He is the First in creation and the Last in provision.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This is the second proof. The first group (al-awwalūn) argue that the verse 'He is the First and the Last' implies that God is the First before everything and the Last after everything, which requires the annihilation of all creatures. Since there is nothing after the Hereafter (i.e., after the final state), the 'Last' must refer to the state before the Hereafter. The refutation offers multiple alternative interpretations (tafsīrāt) for 'al-Awwal' and 'al-Ākhir': (1) al-mabdaʾ (the Beginning) and al-ghāya (the End) meaning the ultimate purpose; (2) He is the Instrument (al-āla) only; (3) He is the Remaining (al-bāqī) after the death of living things; (4) He is the First in creation and the Last in provision. These interpretations show that the verse does not necessarily entail universal annihilation before resurrection.

Key Terms
الأول والآخر (the First and the Last)
المبدأ (the Beginning)
الغاية (the End)
الآلة (the Instrument)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'He is the First and the Last' must mean God is temporally first and last, implying that everything else is annihilated.
Correction: The verse has multiple valid interpretations, such as God being the Beginning and End (source and goal), the Instrument, or the Abiding after death, none of which require annihilation of all creatures.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, which of the following is NOT a possible interpretation of 'al-Awwal wa al-Ākhir'?
A) The Beginning and the End
B) The Instrument only
C) The First in creation and the Last in provision
D) The First in time and the Last in time

Key Takeaway
The verse 'He is the First and the Last' is interpreted in multiple ways, none of which necessarily entail the annihilation of all creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone presents a seemingly strong proof from the Qur'an that forces a conclusion about God's attributes, do you accept it hastily or do you seek the deeper, broader meanings that scholars have drawn? Have you ever been guilty of narrowing God's words to fit your own limited understanding? Repent and commit to seeking deeper knowledge.
?
The verse 'He is the First and the Last' reminds you that everything begins from Him and returns to Him. How does this truth change the way you view your own beginning and end? Are you living as if you are from Him and to Him? Ponder this and set a concrete action to align your life with that reality.
?
The refutation shows that a single verse can have multiple valid meanings. Does this humble you when you interpret the Qur'an? Have you ever been absolutely certain about an interpretation only to discover a broader understanding? Ask yourself: Will you seek to know the diverse meanings of God's names, or remain content with surface understanding? Choose to be a lifelong learner.

Third Proof: 'Everything Perishes Except His Face' and Its Refutation
Presents the verse of universal perishing as proof and argues that possibility itself is perishing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'Everything perishes except His Face' is used as a proof for annihilation and why the refutation argues that possibility itself is a form of perishing.

Core Idea
The verse does not prove total annihilation; contingency itself is a kind of perishing.

This lesson explores the third proof presented by those who argue for universal annihilation before resurrection. They cite the Qur'anic verse 'Everything perishes except His Face.' The refutation, however, introduces a subtle distinction: that the inherent possibility (contingency) of all created things is itself a form of perishing, and the verse may refer to the loss of primary benefit rather than complete non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the structure: the proof, a clarification, and the refutation.
2
Identify the proof's claim
The verse is presented as evidence that everything will be annihilated. What is the initial understanding of 'perish'?
The text says the intended meaning is not removal from benefit.
3
Understand the clarification about benefit
The proponent clarifies that 'perishing' does not mean loss of benefit, because indicating the Maker remains even after scattering.
4
Analyze the two refutation points
The refutation gives two reasons: (1) possibility (contingency) itself is perishing, and (2) loss of the primary benefit for which a thing is created is also perishing, even if it serves another benefit.
Note that not every substance is created solely for indication.
5
Reflect on the implication
The verse does not necessitate absolute annihilation before resurrection; perishing can mean contingency or loss of intended purpose.
This refutation preserves the possibility of resurrection after annihilation or transformation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - قوله تعالى: {كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه} وليس المراد الخروج عن الانتفاع، لأن منفعة الدلالة على الصانع باقية بعد التفرق. وأجيب بأن الإمكان هلاك في نفسه، وكذا الخروج عن الانتفاع الذي خلق الشيء لأجله، وإن صلح لمنفعة أخرى، وليس خلق كل جوهر للاستدلال.
Third - His statement, the Exalted: 'Everything perishes except His Face' and the intended meaning is not removal from benefit, because the benefit of indicating the Maker remains after scattering. And it was answered that possibility is perishing in itself, and likewise removal from the benefit for which the thing was created, even if it is suitable for another benefit, and not every substance is created for indication.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This is the third proof. The first group argues that the verse 'Every thing is perishing except His Face' indicates universal annihilation. They clarify that what is meant by 'perishing' is not merely the loss of benefit, because the benefit of indicating the Creator remains even after the parts are scattered. The refutation presents two points: (1) possibility (al-imkān) – i.e., the inherent contingency of all created things – is in itself a kind of perishing (halāk), meaning that their contingent nature is enough to consider them 'perishing' without actual annihilation. (2) 'Perishing' can also refer to the loss of the primary benefit for which the thing was created, even if it could serve some other benefit. Moreover, not every substance is created solely for the purpose of indication (istidlāl) of the Maker. Thus, the verse does not necessitate absolute annihilation before resurrection.

Key Terms
هالك (perishing)
وجهه (His Face)
الإمكان (possibility)
الانتفاع (benefit)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Everything perishes except His Face' proves that all creatures will cease to exist entirely before resurrection.
Correction: The refutation shows that 'perishing' can refer to the inherent contingency of creatures or the loss of their primary purpose, not necessarily absolute non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the third proof, what does 'possibility' (al-imkān) mean in this context?
A) That things are possible to annihilate
B) That things are inherently contingent and thus considered 'perishing' in themselves
C) That God has the power to annihilate everything
D) That only the face of God is eternal

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Everything perishes except His Face' does not prove absolute annihilation; the inherent contingency of creatures and the loss of primary purpose both constitute perishing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what specific area of your life do you still act as if something other than God is permanent? What would change if you truly believed that even your own existence is 'perishing in itself' every moment? Commit today to recite 'Everything perishes except His Face' with that understanding and let go of one attachment you treat as eternal.
?
The passage says that removal from the benefit for which a thing is created counts as perishing. What has God created you for? Honestly, are you living for that purpose, or are you wasting your 'primary benefit' on other pursuits? Write down one concrete change you will make this week to align with your purpose.
?
Consider that the possibility of your existence is itself a form of non-being. How does that humble you? Do you live with the awareness that you are ontologically nothing without God? What is one practical habit you can start today to remind yourself of your constant dependence on Him?

Fourth Proof: 'He Begins Creation then Returns It' and Its Refutation
Presents a proof and its refutation regarding resurrection from non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic argument linking resurrection to initial creation from non-existence and its refutation by noting that initial creation was from existing matter.

Core Idea
Resurrection need not be from absolute nothingness; creation began from clay.

This lesson presents the fourth proof of a group that argues for annihilation before resurrection based on Quranic verses about beginning and returning creation. The refutation shows that the analogy fails because initial creation was from preexisting matter, not nothing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Note the flow from proof to refutation.
The passage is concise: one verse pair for the proof, another for the refutation.
2
Identify the argument and counter-argument
Identify the claim that resurrection must be from non-existence because creation was from nothing, and the counter using the clay verse.
3
Reflect on the logical point
Consider why the refutation is effective: it shows that 'beginning' does not always imply absolute non-existence.
This refutes the necessity of annihilation before resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - قوله تعالى: {وهو الذي يبدأ الخلق ثم يعيده} {كما بدأنا أول خلق نعيده} والبدء من العدم، فكذا العود. وأجيب بأن بدء الخلق قد لا يكون عن عدم، قال الله تعالى: {وبدأ خلق الإنسان من طين}.
Fourth - His statement, the Exalted: 'It is He who begins creation then returns it' and 'As We began the first creation, We will return it' - and the beginning is from non-existence, so similarly the return. And it was answered that the beginning of creation may not be from non-existence, God said, the Exalted: 'And He began the creation of man from clay.'
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This is the fourth proof. The first group uses the verses that compare the beginning of creation with its return (resurrection). They argue that just as the initial creation was from non-existence (al-ʿadam), so too the return (al-ʿawd) must be from non-existence – implying annihilation before resurrection. The refutation points out that the beginning of creation is not always from absolute nothingness. The verse cited: 'And He began the creation of man from clay' (Q. 32:7) indicates that the original creation of Adam was from existing matter (clay). Therefore, the analogy does not require a prior state of non-existence. Thus, resurrection could be a reassembly of existing material rather than recreation after annihilation.

Key Terms
البدء (beginning)
العدم (non-existence)
العود (return)
طين (clay)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The beginning of creation is always from absolute non-existence.
Correction: The Quran states that the beginning of man was from clay, showing that creation can begin from existing matter.

Checkpoint

C) {وبدأ خلق الإنسان من طين}
D) {وخلق الإنسان من طين}

Key Takeaway
The Quranic analogy between creation and resurrection does not require annihilation because initial creation involved preexisting matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Fifth Proof: The Verse of Perishing and Its Refutation
Presents verse and refutes by distinguishing loss of benefit from annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'Everyone upon it will perish' is interpreted by the opponents of bodily resurrection and how the refutation redefines 'perishing' as loss of benefit, not absolute non-existence.

Core Idea
Perishing often means losing usefulness, not ceasing to exist.

The fifth proof against bodily resurrection cites the Qur'anic verse 'Everyone upon it will perish' (Q. 55:26). At first glance, it seems to indicate that all beings are annihilated. The refutation, however, draws on a linguistic and conceptual distinction: 'perishing' (fanā') can simply mean the loss of intended benefit, as in 'the food perished' or 'the war destroyed them.' This lesson examines the proof and its response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the refutation is introduced with the word 'وأجيب' (and it was answered).
2
Identify the core argument
The opponents use the verse to argue for universal annihilation. Find the exact wording.
3
Analyze the refutation
The refutation claims that 'perishing' can mean loss of intended benefit. Consider the examples given: provision perishing and war destroying people. How do these illustrate the point?
This is a linguistic argument, not a denial of the verse.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter for the doctrine of bodily resurrection?
It shows that the verse does not necessarily negate bodily resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس - قوله تعالى: {كل من عليها فان} وأجيب بأن الفناء قد يكون بالخروج عن الانتفاع المقصود مثل: فني الزاد والطعام، وأفناهم الحرب.
Fifth - His statement, the Exalted: 'Everyone upon it will perish' and it was answered that perishing may be by removal from the intended benefit, like 'the provision and food perished' and 'the war destroyed them.'
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This is the fifth proof. The first group cites the verse 'Everyone upon it (the earth) will perish' (Q. 55:26) to argue for universal annihilation. The refutation explains that 'perishing' (fanāʾ) can mean the loss of intended benefit (intifāʿ maqṣūd), not necessarily literal non-existence. Two examples are given: (1) 'the provision and food perished' – meaning they were consumed or lost their usefulness, not that they ceased to exist absolutely; (2) 'the war destroyed them' – meaning they were killed or rendered ineffective, but their substances may still exist. Thus, the verse does not necessitate absolute annihilation before resurrection.

Key Terms
فان (perishing)
الفناء (annihilation)
الانتفاع (benefit)
الزاد (provision)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every occurrence of 'fanāʾ' in Arabic means absolute non-existence.
Correction: The passage shows that 'fanāʾ' can refer to loss of intended benefit, as in 'the provision perished' or 'the war destroyed them.'

Checkpoint
What is the key point in the refutation of the fifth proof?
A) The verse only applies to non-believers
B) 'Perishing' can mean losing intended benefit, not ceasing to exist
C) The verse was abrogated by other verses
D) 'Perishing' refers to the soul, not the body

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Everyone upon it will perish' does not necessarily imply absolute annihilation; 'perishing' can mean loss of benefit, leaving room for bodily resurrection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Are you clinging to any 'benefit' in this world as if it were permanent? How can you shift your attachment from temporary utilities to the enduring reality of resurrection, knowing that Allah has promised a new creation?

Two Views on Resurrection and the Choice of Tawaqquf
Describes the two views on resurrection and endorses tawaqquf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two major positions on how bodily resurrection occurs and why the author opts for suspending judgment (tawaqquf) due to lack of decisive evidence.

Core Idea
Bodily resurrection is certain, but its mode is left undecided.

In this passage, the author presents the two prevailing views on the mechanism of bodily resurrection: either God creates the body anew after annihilation, or He reassembles the dispersed parts. After presenting both, he takes the position of tawaqquf—suspending judgment—following Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni. Let's explore why the author considers this the most intellectually honest stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the terms used for the two views.
2
Identify the two views
The passage mentions two differing opinions among those who affirm bodily resurrection: i'ada bi ijad ba'd al-fana (re-creation after annihilation) and jam' ba'd tafarruq al-ajza' (reassembly after dispersal). Understand each.
These correspond to creation ex nihilo after destruction vs. reconstruction from scattered parts.
3
Understand tawaqquf
The author states that the truth is tawaqquf (suspension) and cites Imam al-Haramayn. Note the reasoning: both are intellectually possible, and no decisive scriptural proof (qati' sam'i) determines which occurs.
Tawaqquf is not disbelief; it's epistemic humility in the absence of clear evidence.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the author does not choose one view over the other. This teaches us about the role of reason and revelation in kalam: when revelation is ambiguous, reason leaves room for both possibilities.
This passage guards against dogmatism on issues where no decisive proof exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني أن القائلين بصحة الفناء، وبحقية حشر الأجساد اختلفوا في أن ذلك بإيجاد بعد الفناء، أو بالجمع بعد تفرق الأجزاء، والحق التوقف، وهو اختيار إمام الحرمين، حيث قال : يجوز عقلاً أن تعدم الجواهر، ثم تعاد، وأن تبقى وتزول أعراضها المعهودة ثم تعاد بنيتها، ولم يدل قاطع سمعي على تعيين أحدهما . فلا يبعد أن يغير أجسام العباد على صفة أجسام التراب، ثم يعاد تركيبها إلى ما عهد، ولا نحيل أن يعدم منها شيء، ثم يعاد ، والله أعلم.
Meaning that those who affirm the validity of annihilation and the truth of the gathering of bodies differed: is that by creation after annihilation, or by gathering after the dispersal of parts? And the truth is suspension (tawaqquf), and it is the choice of Imam al-Haramayn, who said: It is intellectually permissible that substances be annihilated and then restored, and that they remain while their accustomed accidents cease and then their structure is restored. No decisive scriptural proof (qati' sam'i) indicates which of the two. So it is not unlikely that the bodies of servants be changed to the state of dust, then their composition be restored as accustomed. Nor do we deem it impossible that something from them be annihilated and then restored. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage defines the two positions regarding the resurrection of bodies: either God creates the body anew after annihilation (i'ada bi ijad ba'd al-fana) or He gathers the dispersed parts and reassembles them (jam' ba'd tafarruq al-ajza'). The author states that the correct view is to suspend judgment (tawaqquf), which is the choice of Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni. He then explains Imam al-Haramayn's position: both are intellectually possible—substances could cease to exist and then be recreated, or they could persist while their accidents change. Since no decisive scriptural evidence determines which mode actually occurs, one should not rule out either possibility. The passage ends with a statement of trust in God's knowledge.

Key Terms
فناء
حشر الأجساد
توقف
إمام الحرمين
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author affirms a specific mode of resurrection (e.g., creation after annihilation).
Correction: The author explicitly says 'the truth is suspension' (tawaqquf) and does not choose either view, following Imam al-Haramayn's reasoning that no decisive proof exists.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the author's position on how resurrection occurs?
A) It is definitely by creation after annihilation.
B) It is definitely by reassembly of parts.
C) He suspends judgment (tawaqquf).
D) He rejects the possibility of bodily resurrection.

Key Takeaway
The mode of bodily resurrection is not decisively known; therefore, the pious stance is to suspend judgment (tawaqquf) while affirming the resurrection itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your own faith do you find yourself insisting on a specific explanation when the texts remain silent? Are you willing to adopt tawaqquf and trust that God's knowledge is sufficient?
?
When faced with two equally plausible possibilities, does your heart rest in uncertainty or does it demand a definitive answer? How can you cultivate contentment with not knowing?
?
The author defers to 'And God knows best.' When was the last time you truly surrendered a theological question to God without insisting on closure? What would it look like for you to practice tawaqquf in matters where you lack decisive evidence?

The Third Proof: Everything Perishes Except His Face
States the third proof and its initial objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face' is used as a proof for annihilation and the initial objection regarding benefit.

Core Idea
The verse proves annihilation, but the intended meaning is not merely loss of benefit.

Sanusi presents the third proof for the annihilation of the world by citing the Quranic verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face.' An objector might argue that the verse refers to loss of benefit, not absolute non-existence, since even after dispersal, things serve as evidence for the Creator. This lesson unpacks the proof and the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof from the Quran
Start by reading the Arabic text of the proof: the verse and its straightforward application. Notice how the verse is taken as evidence for the annihilation of all things except Allah.
2
Identify the objection
After the proof, an objection is raised: the verse may not mean complete annihilation but rather the end of a thing's benefit. The objector argues that the benefit of indicating the Creator remains even after dissolution.
This objection is crucial for understanding the need for a deeper answer.
3
Reflect on the strength of the objection
Consider why the objector's point is plausible: many things outlive their primary use and still have secondary purposes. How does this challenge the proof?

Source Text

The Answer: Perishing as Loss of Intended Benefit
Provides the answer to the objection on perishing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam answer that perishing means either possibility (imkan) in itself or exit from the intended benefit, even if the thing has other uses.

Core Idea
Perishing is defined as inherent possibility or loss of intended benefit, not absolute non-existence.

The answer to the previous objection defines what 'perishing' means in the context of the verse. Sanusi explains that possibility (imkan) is itself a form of perishing, and likewise exiting the benefit for which the thing was created counts as perishing, even if the thing remains useful for another purpose. This lesson explores this nuanced theological answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer text

2

The answer gives two interpretations: (1) possibility (imkan) is perishing in itself, meaning that anything contingent is inherently liable to cease; (2) exit from the intended benefit is also perishing, even if the thing is suitable for another benefit.
The second meaning addresses the objection: even if a thing continues to serve as evidence, it has perished relative to its original purpose.
3
Note the incomplete ending
The passage ends abruptly with 'and not every essence is created...' The full answer may continue, possibly to clarify exceptions. Consider how this might modify the argument.
This is a reminder that the text may be incomplete; refer to the original source if needed.

The Fourth Proof for Resurrection and Its Refutation
Presents the fourth proof and its answer regarding resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof for resurrection based on God's beginning and repeating creation, and the answer that shows beginning is not necessarily from nothing.

Core Idea
Resurrection's proof from creation's repeat is refuted by man's creation from clay.

This passage presents a scriptural proof for resurrection and its counterargument. The proof uses the idea that God begins creation from nothing and repeats it, so resurrection is similar. The answer points out that the initial creation of man was from existing matter (clay), not absolute nothing, thus the resurrection may involve reassembly rather than creation ex nihilo. Understanding this nuance is key to appreciating the theological debate on resurrection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise style of the argument and response.
The passage is short but contains a full proof and answer.
2
Understand the proof's logic
The proof argues: God begins creation from nothing and repeats it, so resurrection (the repeat) must also be from nothing. Identify the two parts: the verse and the deduction.
The key is the assumption that 'beginning' is from absolute non-existence.
3
Grasp the answer's nuance
The answer refutes by citing God's creation of man from clay—showing that beginning can be from preexistent matter. Reflect on how this weakens the proof for resurrection from nothing.
This opens the possibility of resurrection as reassembly of parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - قوله تعالى : وهو الذي يبدأ الخلق ثم يعيده (٢) كما بدأنا أول خلق نعيده (۳) والبدء من العدم، فكذا العود. وأجيب بأن بدء الخلق قد لا يكون عن عدم، قال الله تعالى: ﴿ وبدأ خلق الإنسان من طين) (٤) .
Fourth: His statement the Exalted: 'And He is the One who begins creation then repeats it' (30:27), 'As We began the first creation We will repeat it' (21:104). And the beginning is from non-existence, so likewise the return. And it was answered: That the beginning of creation may not be from non-existence, God the Exalted said: 'And He began the creation of man from clay' (32:7).
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for resurrection as a new creation after annihilation. The argument: God begins creation from nothing ('adam), so the return (resurrection) must also be from nothing. The answer refutes this by noting that the beginning of creation may not be from absolute non-existence; for example, the creation of man began from clay, which preexisted. Thus, beginning from preexistent matter is possible, and so resurrection could be from preexistent parts (reassembly) rather than from nothing.

Key Terms

أعاد
بدء من العدم
