Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think the verse 'He begins creation then repeats it' necessarily proves resurrection from absolute nothing, as the first creation was from nothing.
Correction: The answer shows that the first creation of man was from clay (preexistent matter), so 'beginning' can involve preexisting material. Thus the repeat could also involve reassembly, not necessarily from nothing.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof for resurrection is weakened by the recognition that the beginning of creation can involve preexistent matter, as seen in the creation of man from clay.

The Fifth Proof: Annihilation as Loss of Benefit
Sanusi presents the fifth proof (Quran verse) and its response, clarifying 'fana' as loss of benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse 'Everyone upon it is perishing' was interpreted as not necessarily implying absolute annihilation, but rather the loss of intended benefit.

Core Idea
Fana can mean loss of benefit, not loss of being.

The fifth proof from the Quran states that all beings are perishing (fana). But what does 'fana' really mean? A careful answer shows that it may refer to leaving the intended benefit rather than annihilation into non-existence. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and answer
Read the Arabic verse and its translation. Notice the structure: proof then answer.
The proof is the verse, the answer is the response.
2
Identify the key term 'fana'
Understand that 'fana' has two possible meanings: absolute annihilation or loss of intended benefit.
The answer clarifies which meaning applies here.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the examples given: food perishing and war perishing. How do they illustrate 'loss of intended benefit' without loss of existence?
Food still exists as matter; war casualties still exist as souls.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider that substances may remain even when their functions are lost. This affects how we interpret scriptural statements about annihilation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس - قوله تعالى : كل من عليها فان (٥) . وأجيب بأن الفناء قد يكون بالخروج عن الانتفاع المقصود مثل: فني الزاد والطعام، وأفناهم الحرب .)

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the Quranic verse 'Everyone upon it (the earth) is perishing' (Rahman 55:26) that all beings undergo annihilation. The answer counters that 'fana' (perishing) may simply mean losing the intended benefit, not absolute non-existence. Examples are given: food perishes (becomes inedible) but still exists as matter, and war destroys people but they do not become non-existent in the philosophical sense. Thus the verse does not necessitate complete annihilation of substances.

The First Proof: Consensus and Its Refutation
Presents the proof of consensus and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for annihilation (consensus of early Muslims) and why it is refuted by the contrary consensus of the Baghdad Mu'tazila and the limited scope of the Companions' agreement.

Core Idea
Consensus on annihilation is claimed but refuted—the Companions agreed on observable perishing, not philosophical non-existence.

This lesson examines the first of two proofs traditionally offered for the annihilation of all creation: the claim of a consensus (ijma') from the early period before dissenters appeared. We will see how this argument was challenged and why the Companions' agreement, though real, does not support the technical doctrine of absolute non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: proof, rebuttal, clarification.
The passage is dense but follows a clear argumentative structure.
2
Identify the two groups
The passage mentions 'some later Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna' who claimed consensus, and the 'Mu'tazila of Baghdad' who opposed. Understand how this breaks the claim of universal consensus.
3
Distinguish the two meanings of annihilation
The Companions agreed on annihilation as perishing, death, and dispersal of parts, not as the complete non-existence of substances. This distinction is key to the rebuttal.
The Companions did not delve into philosophical subtleties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول - الإجماع على ذلك قبل ظهور المخالفين، كبعض المتأخرين من المعتزلة، وأهل السنة ورد بالمنع كيف وقد أطبقت معتزلة بغداد على خلافه . نعم، كان الصحابة مجمعين على بقاء الحق وفناء الخلق، بمعنى هلاك الأشياء، وموت الأحياء، وتفرق الأجزاء، لا بمعنى انعدام الجواهر بالكلية ، لأن الظاهر أنهم لم يكونوا يخوضون في هذه التدقيقات.
First: Consensus (ijma') on that before the appearance of opponents, like some later Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna. And it was refuted by denial: How, when the Mu'tazila of Baghdad unanimously agreed on the contrary? Indeed, the Companions were unanimous on the survival of the Truth and the annihilation of creation, meaning the perishing of things, death of living beings, and dispersal of parts, not meaning the complete non-existence of substances, because apparently they did not delve into these subtleties.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof used by those who affirm annihilation: consensus (ijma') before the emergence of dissenters. The answer refutes this by pointing out that the Baghdad school of Mu'tazila unanimously disagreed with this claim. However, it concedes that the Companions (al-sahaba) were agreed on the survival of God (al-Haqq) and the annihilation of creation, but this annihilation is understood as perishing of things, death, and dispersal of parts, not as the absolute non-existence (in'dam) of substances. The Companions did not engage in these philosophical subtleties, so their consensus cannot be taken as evidence for the technical annihilation of substances.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The early consensus of Muslims proves that the universe will completely cease to exist (non-existence of substances).
Correction: The passage clarifies that the Companions' consensus referred to observable perishing, not philosophical non-existence, and that the Baghdad Mu'tazila opposed the very claim of consensus.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The claimed consensus for the annihilation of substances fails because the Baghdad Mu'tazila opposed it, and the Companions' consensus only covers observable perishing, not philosophical non-existence.

?

The Second Proof: He is the First and the Last
Presents the second proof (verse) and its answer regarding annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from the verse 'He is the First and the Last' and how Sunni theologians interpret it non-temporally to avoid implying annihilation of all things.

Core Idea
God is First and Last in a timeless sense, not as a temporal conclusion.

This lesson examines the second proof cited by some theologians: the Quranic verse 'He is the First and the Last.' At first glance, the verse might suggest that God alone remains after everything else is annihilated. However, Sunni scholars have offered multiple interpretations that preserve the eternity of Paradise and avoid the problematic conclusion. Let's explore the argument and its responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and initial argument
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note how the verse is used to argue that for God to be the First and Last in existence, everything else must be annihilated.
The argument relies on a literal temporal understanding of 'first' and 'last'.
2
Identify the problem with the literal interpretation
Observe that the argument concludes that annihilation occurs before resurrection, but this contradicts the eternal nature of Paradise. Understand why this is problematic.
The objection is based on the agreed fact that Paradise is eternal.
3
Examine the alternative interpretations
Go through each of the four interpretations provided in the answer. Note how each avoids a temporal reading and instead focuses on priority in rank, uniqueness, or sustenance.
These interpretations are non-exhaustive but show the flexibility of the verse's meaning.
4
Grasp the overarching conclusion
Understand that the verse's intended meaning is not temporal finality. The summary clarifies that all Muslims agree on the eternity of Paradise, so the literal temporal reading is invalid.
The key is that the verse must be interpreted in a way consistent with other established doctrines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني قوله تعالى : هو الأول والآخر ) أي في الوجود. ولا يتصور ذلك إلا بانعدام ما سواه. وليس بعد القيامة وفاقاً. فيكون قبلها . وأجيب بأنه يجوز أن يكون المعنى هو مبدأ كل موجود، وغاية كل مقصود، أو هو المتوحد في الألوهية أو صفات الكمال ، كما إذا قيل لك : أهذا أول من زارك أو آخرهم؟ فتقول : هو الأول والآخر، وتريد أنه لا زائر سواه . أو هو الأول والآخر بالنسبة إلى كل حي . بمعنى أنه يبقى بعد موت جميع الأحياء. أو هو الأول خلقاً والآخر رزقاً كما قال : خلقكم ثم رزقكم (۲) وبالجملة فليس المراد أنه آخر كل شيء بحسب الزمان للاتفاق على أبدية الجنة من فيها .
Second: His statement the Exalted: 'He is the First and the Last' (57:3), i.e., in existence. And that cannot be conceived except by the annihilation of what is other than Him. And it is not after the resurrection by agreement. So it is before it. And it was answered: That it is possible that the meaning is: He is the origin of every existent and the end of every purpose, or He is the unique in divinity or attributes of perfection, just as if it is said to you: 'Is this the first of your visitors or the last?' and you say: 'He is the first and the last,' meaning there is no visitor other than him. Or it is the first and the last with respect to every living thing, meaning that He remains after the death of all living beings. Or He is the first in creating and the last in providing sustenance, as He said: 'He created you then provided for you' (30:40). In summary, the intended meaning is not that He is the last of everything in terms of time, because of agreement on the eternity of Paradise and those in it.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the Quranic verse 'He is the First and the Last' (Hadid 57:3). The argument: To be the First and Last in existence requires that everything else be annihilated (except God) so that He alone remains. Since this does not happen after resurrection (Paradise is eternal), it must happen before resurrection, implying annihilation of all things. The answer provides multiple alternative interpretations: (1) 'First' means the origin of all existents, 'Last' means the ultimate purpose. (2) It is a qualitative uniqueness, like saying a person is the only visitor. (3) 'First' and 'Last' with respect to living beings: He remains after all die. (4) He is first in creating and last in providing sustenance. The answer concludes by noting that the verse cannot mean temporal finality because Paradise and its inhabitants are eternal, so the interpretation must be non-temporal.

Key Terms
هو الأول والآخر
انعدام ما سواه
أبدية الجنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'He is the First and the Last' means that at some point in time only God will exist after annihilation of everything else.
Correction: The verse can be interpreted non-temporally, as God being the origin and ultimate purpose of everything, or unique in divinity, or remaining after all living beings die, etc.

Checkpoint
What interpretation of 'He is the First and the Last' does the answer use to avoid implying annihilation of all things?
A) He is first in time and last in time.
B) He is the origin and ultimate purpose of everything.
C) He is first in creating humans and last in judging them.
D) He is the first to exist and the last to perish.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'He is the First and the Last' is understood in a non-temporal sense to avoid the conclusion of annihilation, with multiple valid interpretations preserving divine uniqueness and the eternity of Paradise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'He is the First and the Last' in your daily prayers, have you ever felt a subtle fear that everything, even Paradise, will eventually end? How does this lesson's reinterpretation of the verse transform that fear into a firm hope in the eternity of the Hereafter? What practical step can you take today to let that hope shape your actions?
?
The proof argued that if God is First and Last in a literal temporal sense, everything else must be annihilated. How does knowing that God's being does not require your annihilation, and that Paradise is eternal, change the way you view your own life and purpose? Third Proof Revisited: Two Interpretations of 'Everything Perishes Except His Face'
Presents the third proof again with a different answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two possible interpretations of the Qur'anic verse 'Everything perishes except His Face' (28:88) used as a proof for the contingency of the world, and how it avoids the implication of absolute annihilation.

Core Idea
The verse does not prove absolute annihilation but either essential perishing or loss of function.

This passage presents a nuanced reading of the well-known verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face.' The proof from this verse is a classic argument for the contingency of the world, but it requires careful interpretation to avoid implying that substances cease to exist. The author offers two alternative explanations for what 'halik' (perishing) means, preserving the proof while answering a common objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its literal English translation to get the full argument in its original form.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'halik' and 'wajh'.
2
Identify the proof and its initial interpretation
Notice that the proof begins by stating the verse and clarifying that the intended meaning is annihilation (in'dam).
This sets up the objection that follows.
3
Understand the objection
The objection argues that the verse cannot mean mere loss of benefit because even after a thing is scattered, it still serves as a proof of the Creator.
This forces a more precise definition of 'perishing'.
4
Examine the two answers
The passage provides two possible interpretations of 'halik': (1) perishing in itself due to contingency, and (2) loss of intended benefit suitable to its state, illustrated by the example of food.
Each answer avoids the implication that things become absolutely non-existent.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how these interpretations strengthen the argument for the world's contingency while remaining faithful to the Qur'anic text.
This shows the precision of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - قوله تعالى: كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه (۳) فإن المراد به الانعدام، لا الخروج عن كونه منتفعاً به لأن الشيء بعد التفرق يبقى دليلاً على الصانع ، وذلك من أعظم المنافع . وأجيب بأن المعنى أنه هالك في حد ذاته لكونه ممكناً لا يستحق الوجود إلا بالنظر إلى العلة أو المراد بالهلاك الموت أو الخروج عن الانتفاع المقصود به اللائق بحاله . كما يقال : هلك الطعام إذالم يبق صالحاً للأكل، وإن صلح لمنفعة .
Third: His statement the Exalted: 'Everything is perishing except His Face' (28:88). For the intended meaning is annihilation (in'dam), not exit from being benefited by it, because the thing after dispersal remains a proof of the Maker, and that is among the greatest benefits. And it was answered: That the meaning is that it is perishing in itself, because it is contingent (mumkin) and does not deserve existence except in relation to the cause, or the intended meaning of perishing is death or exit from the intended benefit suitable to its state. As it is said: The food perished when it is no longer fit for eating, even if it is fit for another benefit.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents a second version of the third proof from the verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face' (Qasas 28:88). The proof argues that the verse means absolute annihilation (in'dam), not merely loss of benefit, because even after the thing is dispersed, it still serves as evidence for the Creator, which is a great benefit. The answer provides two possible interpretations: (1) The thing is perishing in itself because it is contingent (mumkin) and only has existence due to its cause; in itself, it is non-existent. (2) The perishing can refer to death or loss of the intended benefit suitable to its state, using the example of food that perishes when it is no longer edible, even if it can be used for other purposes. Thus, the verse does not necessarily entail absolute annihilation of substances.

Key Terms
كل شيء هالك
وجهه
انعدام
إمكان
هلاك الطعام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face' means all things will be annihilated (absolute non-existence).
Correction: The intended meaning is either (1) perishing in themselves due to contingency, or (2) loss of intended benefit, not absolute annihilation.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what does 'halik' (perishing) primarily mean in this context?
A) Absolute non-existence.
B) Loss of benefit intended for the thing.
C) Being in need of a cause.
D) Both B and C are mentioned.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Everything perishes except His Face' is interpreted in two ways: either as essential perishing due to contingency, or as loss of intended benefit, both preserving the proof without implying the absolute non-existence of substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything except Allah's Face is perishing in itself, what does that mean for the things you rely on most? How would your life change if you truly lived with this awareness every moment?
?
The passage uses the example of food that is no longer fit to eat but still has another use. Consider something in your life you consider 'ruined' or 'lost.' How might it still serve a purpose from Allah's perspective? What would it take for you to see it that way?
?
Contingent beings do not deserve existence except through their Cause. Do you live as though you deserve anything from Allah? What would it look like to live with total dependence, expecting nothing but being grateful for everything?

Consensus on Annihilation: Argument and Rebuttal
Taftazani presents and rebuts the consensus argument for fana'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from consensus for the annihilation of the world is rebutted by highlighting the precise meaning of 'fana' and the lack of universal agreement.

Core Idea
Consensus on annihilation does not mean absolute non-existence.

In this passage, Taftazani presents the first argument for the annihilation of the world: consensus (ijma'). But he immediately rebuts it by showing that the alleged consensus is neither universal nor about complete non-existence. This lesson unpacks the rebuttal and clarifies what the early Muslims actually agreed upon.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and rebuttal
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the rebuttal is introduced with 'رد بالمنع' (rebutted by denial).
2
Identify the two meanings of fana'
The passage distinguishes between destruction (halak, death, dispersion) and complete non-existence (in'idam al-jawahir). Understand this key distinction.
This is the core of the rebuttal.
3
Consider why this rebuttal matters
Ask yourself: How does this rebuttal protect a sound theological understanding of the afterlife? The Companions' consensus supports the end of this world but leaves room for the metaphysical details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول - الإجماع على ذلك قبل ظهور المخالفين، كبعض المتأخرين من المعتزلة، وأهل السنة ورد بالمنع كيف وقد أطبقت معتزلة بغداد على خلافه . نعم، كان الصحابة مجمعين على بقاء الحق وفناء الخلق، بمعنى هلاك الأشياء، وموت الأحياء، وتفرق الأجزاء، لا بمعنى انعدام الجواهر بالكلية ، لأن الظاهر أنهم لم يكونوا يخوضون في هذه التدقيقات.
First: Consensus on that before the appearance of dissenters, like some later Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna, is rebutted by denial: how? For the Mu'tazila of Baghdad were unanimous on the contrary. Yes, the Companions were unanimous on the remaining of the Truth (al-Haqq) and the annihilation of creation (fana' al-khalq), meaning the destruction of things, the death of living beings, and the dispersion of parts, not meaning the complete non-existence of atoms (an'idam al-jawahir bi al-kulliya), because it is apparent that they did not delve into these precise investigations.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the annihilation of the world and its rebuttal. The argument claims consensus (ijma') among early Muslims that the world will perish, and that this consensus should be authoritative. However, the rebuttal states that this consensus is invalid because the Mu'tazila of Baghdad, who were a significant group, disagreed with this view, indicating disagreement. Moreover, the author clarifies that the Companions' consensus was only about the destruction of things (hulak, death, dispersion) rather than complete non-existence of atoms (jawahir). They did not engage in such detailed philosophical investigations. Therefore, the argument from consensus fails.

Correction: The passage clarifies that the Companions meant destruction, death, and dispersion, not the complete non-existence of atoms. They did not delve into such precise investigations.

Checkpoint

B) The Mu'tazila of Baghdad disagreed with it.
C) The argument is based on a misinterpretation of the Quran.
D) The consensus was only about the Resurrection.

Key Takeaway
The Companions agreed on the destruction and death of creatures, not on the complete non-existence of atoms, as they did not engage in such metaphysical inquiries.

The Fourth Objection: Beginning and Return of Creation
Al-Taftazani presents the fourth objection and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against the Ash'arite view on the return of creation and how Al-Taftazani answers it by reinterpreting the meaning of 'beginning' as composition rather than creation from nothing.

Core Idea
The return of creation does not require intervening non-existence; 'beginning' means composition, not creation from nothing.

The fourth objection challenges the Ash'arite understanding of bodily resurrection by citing verses that speak of Allah beginning and repeating creation. The objector assumes that 'beginning' means creating from nothing, so the return must also be from nothing, implying a period of non-existence. Al-Taftazani rebuts this by showing that 'beginning' in these verses means composition and assembly, not absolute origination. Let's examine the passage to see how he supports this interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer.
2
Identify the objector's argument
The objector cites three Qur'anic verses and infers that since 'beginning' is from non-existence, 'return' must also be from non-existence, implying the creation is annihilated and then recreated.
3
Understand the answer's reinterpretation
Al-Taftazani answers by redefining 'beginning of creation' as composition and assembly, not bringing from non-existence. He supports this with the verse about creating man from clay and other verses describing creation as observable.
4
Note the refutation of the alternative claim
He dismisses the claim that 'khalq' (creation) means composition, citing linguists who affirm it means origination, whether from a material or not.
This strengthens the main answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - قوله تعالى : وهو الذي يبدأ الخلق ثم يعيده ) (٢) كما بدأنا أول خلق نعيده ) (۳) كما بدأكم تعودون ) (٤) . والبدء من العدم، فكذا العود وايضاً إعادة الخلق بعد إبدائه لا تتصور بدون تخلل العدم. وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم أن المراد بإبداء الخلق الإيجاد والإخراج عن العدم، بل الجمع والتركيب على ما يشعر به قوله : ( وبدأ خلق الإنسان من طين) (٥) ولهذا يوصف بكونه مرئياً مشاهداً كقوله: (أو لم يروا كيف يبدأ الله الخلق (٦) قل سيروا في الأرض فانظروا كيف بدأ الخلق (۷) وأما القول بأن الخلق حقيقة في التركيب تمسكاً بمثل قوله تعالى : خلقكم من تراب (۸) أي ركبكم. ويخلقون إفكا) (۹) اي يركبونه ، فلا يكون حقيقة في الايجاد دفعاً للاشتراك، فضعيف جداً لإطباق أهل اللغة على أنه إحداث وإيجاد، مع تقدير سواء كان عن مادة كما في خلقكم من تراب أو بدونه، كما في خلق الله العالم .
The fourth: His saying, the Exalted: 'And He it is who begins the creation then repeats it' (30:27), 'As We began the first creation We shall repeat it' (21:104), 'As He began you, you shall return' (7:29). And the beginning is from non-existence, so likewise the return; moreover, the re-creation after its first creation cannot be conceptualized without the interposition of non-existence. And it was answered: We do not concede that what is meant by 'beginning of creation' is bringing into existence and extraction from non-existence, but rather it is composition and assembly, as suggested by His saying: 'And He began the creation of man from clay' (32:7). For this reason it is described as being visible and witnessed, as in His saying: 'Do they not see how Allah begins creation?' (29:19), 'Say: Travel in the land and see how He began creation' (29:20). As for the claim that 'creation' (al-khalq) is real in the sense of composition, clinging to such verses as His saying: 'He created you from dust' (40:67), meaning 'He composed you', and 'They create falsehood' (29:17), meaning they compose it, so that it is not real in the sense of bringing into existence, to avoid equivocation—this is very weak, due to the consensus of the people of language that it is 'origination and bringing into existence', regardless of whether it is from a material, as in 'He created you from dust', or without it, as in 'Allah created the world'.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is an objection-answer pair. The objector cites three Qur'anic verses that speak of Allah beginning creation and then repeating it. The objector argues that 'beginning' means bringing into existence from nothing, so 'repeating' must also be from nothing, implying a period of non-existence between the two. The answer rejects this interpretation of 'beginning'. Instead, the answer says that 'beginning' here means composition and assembly, not creation from nothing. This is supported by the verse about creating man from clay, which indicates a visible, observable process. The answer also refutes the alternative claim that 'creation' (khalq) is a real term for composition, using verses like 'He created you from dust' meaning 'He composed you' and 'They create falsehood' meaning they compose it. This claim is dismissed as very weak because the consensus of linguists is that 'khalq' means origination and bringing into existence, whether from a material or without it.

Key Terms

العدم
الخلق
التركيب
الإيجاد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'return of creation' in the Qur'an necessarily means that Allah will annihilate the world and then re-create it from nothing.
Correction: The passage shows that 'beginning' in these verses refers to composition and assembly, not creation from nothing, so the return does not require an intervening state of non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the fourth objection, what is the meaning of 'بدء الخلق' (beginning of creation)?
A) Bringing into existence from non-existence
B) Composition and assembly
C) Annihilation
D) Eternal pre-existence

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection is overcome by interpreting 'beginning of creation' as composition, not as origination from nothing, thereby eliminating the need for an intervening non-existence.

The Fifth Objection: The Verse 'Everything on It is Perishing'
Al-Taftazani answers the fifth objection and cites al-Razi's linguistic point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection from the verse 'كل من عليها فان' is answered by reinterpreting 'fanā' and by applying the Arabic grammar rule about the active participle referring to the future.

Core Idea
The verse doesn't prove present annihilation; 'fān' refers to the future.

The fifth objection claims that the verse 'Everything on it is perishing' proves that all things are currently being annihilated. Al-Taftazani responds by redefining 'fanā' and citing al-Razi's linguistic insight that the active participle here indicates the future. This lesson explores the nuanced response and the grammar behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
The objector equates 'fanā' with absolute non-existence. Note that the answer denies this and redefines 'fanā' as removal of a beneficial attribute.
Also note the alternative interpretation as death.
2
Examine al-Razi's conditional concession
Even if we accept 'fanā' as non-existence, the verse cannot be literal now. Ask yourself: why would a literal reading imply everything is already perished?
3
Grasp the grammatical principle
Al-Razi refers to the rule that the active participle (ism al-fā‘il) without time indication is metaphorical for the future. The subject must possess the quality, but persistence is debated.
This is key to interpreting 'fān' as future.
4
Note the disagreement with al-Talkhīṣ
The author of al-Talkhīṣ mistakenly thought the active participle is like the imperfect verb, applying to both present and future. Understand why this would make the verse seem literal rather than requiring interpretation.
This highlights the precision needed in theological language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس - قوله تعالى : ( كل من عليها فان ) (10) والفناء هو العدم. وأجيب بالمنع ، بل هو خروج الشيء عن الصفة التي ينتفع به عندها كما يقال : فني زاد القوم وفني الطعام والشراب ولهذا يستعمل في الموت مثل : أفناهم الحرب . وقيل معنى الآية كل من على وجه الأرض من الأحياء فهو ميت . قال الإمام الرازي (1) : ولو سلم كون الهلاك والفناء بمعنى العدم، فلا بد في الآيتين من تأويل، إذ لو حملنا على ظاهرهما ، لزم كون الكل هالكاً فانياً في الحال، وليس كذلك . . وليس التأويل بكونه آيلاً إلى العدم على ما ذكرتم أولى من التأويل بكونه قابلاً له ، وهذا منه إشارة إلى ما اتفق عليه أئمة العربية من كون اسم الفاعل ونحوه مجازاً في الاستقبال، وأنه لا بد من الاتصاف بالمعنى المشتق منه وإنما الخلاف في أنه هل يشترط بقاء ذلك المعنى؟ وقد توهم صاحب التلخيص أنه كالمضارع مشترك بين الحال والاستقبال. فاعترض بأن حمله على الاستقبال ليس تأويلاً وصرفاً عن الظاهر.
The fifth: His saying, the Exalted: 'Everyone on it is perishing' (55:26). And 'al-fanā' is non-existence. And it was answered by denial: rather, it is the removal of a thing from the attribute by which it benefits, as one says: 'The provision of the people perished (faniya)' and 'the food and drink perished'; for this reason it is used for death, like 'the war annihilated them (afnāhum)'. And it was said: The meaning of the verse is that every living being on the face of the earth is dead. Imam al-Razi said: Even if we concede that 'al-halāk' and 'al-fanā' mean non-existence, both verses require interpretation, for if we take them literally, it would necessitate that everything is presently destroyed and perishing, which is not the case. And interpreting it as 'tending towards non-existence' as you claim is not more likely than interpreting it as 'being capable of non-existence'. This is a reference to what the leading scholars of Arabic have agreed upon: that the active participle (ism al-fā‘il) and the like are metaphorical for the future, and that it is necessary that the subject possess the derived meaning; the disagreement is only whether the persistence of that meaning is required. And the author of al-Talkhīṣ mistakenly thought that the active participle is like the imperfect verb, shared between present and future. So he objected that applying it to the future is not an interpretation or a deviation from the apparent meaning.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is an objection-answer pair followed by a detailed linguistic discussion. The objector cites the verse 'Everyone on it is perishing' and equates 'fanā' with absolute non-existence. The answer denies this, explaining that 'fanā' means losing the beneficial quality, as when provisions are exhausted, and it can also mean death. An alternative interpretation is that the verse means every living being will die. Al-Razi then makes a linguistic point: even if we accept that 'fanā' and 'halāk' mean non-existence, the verses cannot be taken literally because they would imply that everything is already non-existent now, which is false. So they must be interpreted, and interpreting them as referring to the future (something will become non-existent) is no more a departure from the apparent meaning than interpreting them as referring to capacity (something is capable of non-existence). This touches on the Arabic grammar rule that the active participle (ism al-fā‘il) when used without time indication is metaphorical for the future; the possessor must have the quality, but the disagreement is whether it must persist. The author of al-Talkhīṣ incorrectly thought the active participle is like the imperfect verb, equally applicable to present and future. It is objected that taking it as future is not an interpretation but the apparent meaning.

Key Terms
الفناء
العدم
الهلاك
اسم الفاعل
المجاز
الاستقبال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'كل من عليها فان' proves that everything is currently being annihilated now.
Correction: The active participle 'fān' can refer to the future, and 'fanā' itself means loss of beneficial quality, not absolute non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the fifth objection, what does 'al-fanā' primarily mean in the language?
A) Absolute non-existence
B) Removal of a beneficial attribute
C) Sleep
D) Transformation

Key Takeaway
The verse 'كل من عليها فان' does not prove present annihilation because 'fanā' means loss of beneficial quality and the active participle can indicate the future.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'كل من عليها فان', do you truly internalize that you will perish – not at some distant future, but that your time is finite? What would you change today if you believed this verse speaks to you now, not just as a future event?
?
The lesson shows that 'fanā' can mean loss of beneficial quality. Think of the things you rely on daily – your health, wealth, relationships. Which of these has already begun to 'perish' in a way you haven't acknowledged? How can you use what remains to draw closer to the One who does not perish?
?
Al-Razi warns against a literal reading that leads to false conclusions. Are there areas of your faith where you cling to a surface-level understanding without deeper reflection? Can you identify one belief you hold that might need a similar 'ta’wīl' to preserve its truth in your heart?

Two Objections to Resurrection and Their Answers
Al-Taftazani presents two objections to resurrection and answers them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main objections to the doctrine of bodily resurrection raised by the opponents and the Mu'tazila, and the answer provided from the perspective of divine wisdom.

Core Idea
Resurrection objections are answered by affirming divine purposes: grace, majesty, and uniqueness.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents two arguments against the possibility of bodily resurrection. The first, from the opponents, questions the identity of the resurrected body. The second, from the Mu'tazila, argues that annihilation has no purpose. The author dismisses the first as already refuted and provides a substantive answer to the second, highlighting the divine wisdom behind creation and annihilation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Notice the structure: two objections and an answer.
The author uses very concise language.
2
Identify the first objection
The first objection claims that the resurrected body is not identical to the original, so reward or punishment would not reach the same person. Note that this objection is dismissed as weak.
The author says 'وقد عرفت ضعفه' meaning 'you have known its weakness'.
3
Analyze the second objection
The Mu'tazila objection: annihilation has no purpose because it does not benefit anyone and cannot serve as a recompense. Consider why this objection is more substantial.
4
Understand the answer
The answer provides three purposes: grace (luṭf) for the believer, manifestation of divine majesty and self-sufficiency, and uniqueness in permanence and eternity.
These purposes show that annihilation is not pointless.
5
Reflect on the significance
Think about how this answer defends the doctrine of resurrection against rational objections and emphasizes divine wisdom.
This is an example of kalam reasoning on sam'iyyat (matters based on revelation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: احتج الآخرون . احتج الآخرون بوجوه : الأول ـ أن المعاد بعد العدم ليس هو المبتدأ بعينه، فلا يكون الجزاء واصلاً . إلى مستحقه، وقد عرفت ضعفه . الثاني - وهو المعتزلة، أنه لا يتصور في الإعدام غرض، إذ لا منفعة فيه لأحد، ولا يصلح جزاء الفعل. وأجيب بأن من الغرض اللطف للمكلف، وإظهار العظمة والاستغناء، والتفرد بالدوام والبقاء.
He said: The others argued. The others argued with arguments: The first: that the thing returned after non-existence is not the very same as the thing begun, so the recompense does not reach its rightful claimant. And you have known its weakness. The second — and it is the Mu‘tazila — that no purpose can be conceived in annihilation, for there is no benefit in it for anyone, nor is it suitable as a recompense for action. And it was answered: Among the purposes are grace (luṭf) for the responsible being, and manifestation of majesty and self-sufficiency, and uniqueness in permanence and eternity.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents two objections to the resurrection (al-ma‘ād) from the opponents (al-ākharūn), along with their answers. The first objection claims that the resurrected body after non-existence is not identical to the original body, so punishment or reward would not reach the same person. The author dismisses this objection as weak, having already explained its weakness earlier (presumably in previous pages). The second objection comes from the Mu‘tazila, who argue that annihilation (i‘dām) has no purpose because it brings no benefit to anyone and cannot serve as a punishment for an action. The answer provides purposes: (1) grace (luṭf) for the responsible being (al-mukallaf), meaning that annihilation may be a mercy for believers; (2) manifestation of Allah's majesty and independence; (3) the uniqueness of Allah in permanence and eternity. Thus, annihilation does have a purpose in the divine plan.

Key Terms
المعاد
المبتدأ
الجزاء
الإعدام
الغرض
اللطف
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite claim that annihilation has no purpose is logically sound and cannot be refuted.
Correction: The answer shows that annihilation does have purposes: grace for the believer, manifestation of majesty, and uniqueness in permanence. Therefore, it is not pointless.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second (Mu'tazilite) objection, which of the following is mentioned as a purpose of annihilation?
A) Punishment of the wicked
B) Grace for the believer
C) Reward for the obedient
D) Destruction of evil

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite objection that annihilation has no purpose is answered by three divine purposes: grace, manifestation of majesty, and uniqueness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed annihilation has no purpose. Do you sometimes live as if your own trials and death are purposeless? How can you see them as Allah's grace and a manifestation of His majesty? What one perspective will you change today?
?
If Allah's annihilation of the world serves to show His uniqueness and self-sufficiency, what does your attachment to worldly things reveal about your belief in His majesty? Will you loosen your grip on something today?
?
The answer says annihilation is a grace (luṭf) for the responsible being. Are you prepared to meet Allah? If your life were annihilated right now, would you be at peace? What would you want to have changed? Make a concrete commitment before you close this lesson.

The First Argument: Annihilation Prevents Just Requital
Taftazani states the first argument against resurrection by creation after annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against resurrection by re-creation, which claims that annihilation of bodies makes just requital impossible because the resurrected would be only a likeness of the original.

Core Idea
If bodies are annihilated, the resurrected cannot be the same person, so reward and punishment cannot reach the deserving.

The first argument against resurrection by creation after annihilation is built on a logical premise: if the body perishes entirely, the one brought back can only be a copy, not the original. Since identity is required for just requital, the argument holds that such a resurrection is impossible. Taftazani presents this argument before refuting it.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the passage where Taftazani states the first argument. Notice the structure: premise, entailment, and conclusion.
Pay attention to the conditional logic: if annihilation, then no just requital.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument relies on the impossibility of re-creating the identical non-existent (إعادة المعدوم). Find this phrase and understand its role.
This premise is the target of the later refutation.
3
Reflect on the consequence
The argument claims that if the body is annihilated, requital cannot reach the deserving. Why is that a problem for resurrection? Consider the Mu'tazila and the Islamic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهم القائلون بأن حشر الأجساد إنما هو بالجمع بعد التفريق، لا بالإيجاد بعد الانعدام بوجوه: الأول - أنه لو عدمت الأجساد لما كان الجزاء واصلاً إلى مستحقه. واللازم باطل سمعاً عندنا بالنصوص الواردة في أن الله تعالى لا يضيع أجر من أحسن عملاً، وعقلاً عند المعتزلة لما سبق من وجود ثواب المطيع وعقاب العاصي. بيان اللزوم أن المعاد لا يكون هو المبدأ بل مثله، لامتناع إعادة المعدوم بعينه. ورد بالمنع، وقد مر بيان ضعف أدلته، ولو سلم فلا يقوم على من يقول ببقاء الروح والأجزاء الأصلية وإعدام البواقي ثم إيجادها إن لم يكن الثاني هو الأول بعينه، بل مغايراً له في صفة الابتداء والإعادة، أو باعتبار آخر. ولا شك أن العمدة الاستحقاق هو الروح على ما مر. وقد يقرر بأنها لو عدمت لما علم إيصال الجزاء إلى مستحقه، لأنه لا يعلم أن ذلك المحشور هو الأول أعيد بعينه، أم مثل له خلق على صفته؟ أما على تقدير الفناء بالكلية فظاهر، وأما على تقدير بقاء الروح والأجزاء الأصلية فلانعدام التركيب والهيئات والصفات التي بها تمايز المثلين، سيما على قول من يجعل الروح أيضاً من قبيل الأجسام. واللازم منتف لأن الأدلة قائمة على وصول الجزاء إلى المستحق. لا يقال لعل الله تعالى يحفظ الروح والأجزاء الأصلية عن التفرق والانحلال بل الحكمة يقتضي ذلك ليعلم وصول الحق إلى المستحق لأنا نقول: المقصود إبطال رأي من يقول بفناء الأجساد بجميع الأجزاء، بل أجسام العالم بأسرها، ثم الإيجاد، وقد حصل. ولو سلم، فقد علمت أن العمدة في الحشر هو الأجزاء الأصلية لا الفضلية، وقد سلمتم أنها لا تفرق، فضلاً عن الانعدام بالكلية. بل الجواب أن المعلوم بالأدلة هو أن الله يوصل الجزاء إلى المستحق ولا دلالة على أنا نعلم ذلك بالإيصال البتة، وكفى بالله عليماً. ولو سلم، فلعل الله يخلق علماً ضرورياً، أو طريقاً جلياً جزئياً أو كلياً.
And among those who say that the gathering of bodies is only by assembly after separation, not by creation after annihilation, there are aspects: The first — that if bodies were annihilated, requital would not reach the one deserving it. And the consequent is false, according to revelation for us, due to the texts stating that God does not waste the reward of those who do good, and according to reason for the Mu'tazila, due to what preceded about the existence of reward for the obedient and punishment for the disobedient. The explanation of the entailment: the resurrected cannot be the original but rather its like, due to the impossibility of re-creating the identical non-existent. It is refuted by denial, and the weakness of its proofs has already been mentioned. And even if it is granted, it does not apply to one who holds that the soul and the original parts remain, and the rest are annihilated, then brought into existence, if the second is not identical to the first but rather different from it in the attribute of beginning and re-creation, or according to another consideration. And there is no doubt that the foundation of deserving is the soul, as mentioned. And it may be established that if they were annihilated, it would not be known that requital reaches the one deserving it, because it is not known that that gathered one is the first re-created identically, or a likeness created in his description? As for the assumption of total annihilation, it is obvious. As for the assumption of the soul and original parts remaining, it is due to the lack of composition, forms, and attributes by which likenesses are distinguished, especially according to the one who makes the soul also a type of body. And the necessary consequence is negated because the proofs are established that requital reaches the one deserving. It may not be said: perhaps God preserves the soul and original parts from dispersal and dissolution, rather wisdom requires that so that the reaching of the right to the deserving is known. For we say: the aim is to refute the opinion of whoever says that bodies perish entirely, indeed all the bodies of the world, then re-creation, and it has occurred. And even if granted, you have known that the foundation in resurrection is the original parts, not the superfluous ones, and you have conceded that they do not disperse, let alone total annihilation. Rather, the answer is that what is known by the proofs is that God delivers requital to the one deserving, and there is no indication that we know that delivery definitely, and sufficient is God as Knower. And even if granted, perhaps God creates necessary knowledge, or a clear way, partial or total.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument of those who claim that resurrection is by assembly after separation, not by creation after annihilation. The argument states that if bodies were annihilated, just requital could not reach the deserving person, because the resurrected would be a like body, not the identical one, making it impossible for reward or punishment to reach the same individual. This consequent is false both by revelation (clear texts that God does not waste reward) and by reason (for the Mu'tazila, due to the necessity of reward and punishment). The entailment is explained: the resurrected must be a likeness of the original, not the identical thing, because re-creating the identical non-existent is impossible. The refutation first denies the impossibility of re-creating the identical non-existent, noting that the weakness of that proof has already been shown. Even if it were granted, the argument does not apply to those who hold that the soul and the original parts remain while only the superfluous parts are annihilated and then re-created, since the second is not identical in every respect but different in the attribute of beginning and re-creation. The foundation of desert is the soul, as previously stated. If the soul were annihilated, it would be unclear whether the resurrected is the same or a likeness. But on the view of the soul's survival, the problem of distinguishing between two like things arises due to the loss of composition and attributes. This is especially problematic for those who consider the soul a body. However, this consequence is negated because proofs show that requital does reach the deserving. The author then addresses a potential objection: perhaps God preserves the soul and original parts from dispersal to ensure knowledge of just delivery. The response is that the aim is to refute the view of total annihilation of all bodies, including the original parts. If that view is granted, the foundation of resurrection is the original parts, not the superfluous ones, and it is conceded that they do not disperse, let alone become totally non-existent. The final answer is that what is known by evidence is that God delivers requital, and we do not need to know the exact mechanism; God can create necessary knowledge or a clear way to understand it.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم
المعاد
الجزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if bodies are annihilated, God cannot bring back the same person.
Correction: Taftazani will later argue that the soul and essential parts survive, preserving identity.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why would requital not reach the deserving if bodies are annihilated?
A) Because the resurrected body would be a different person.
B) Because God cannot bring back the dead.
C) Because the soul is annihilated.
D) Because the body is not needed for reward.

Key Takeaway
The first argument against resurrection by re-creation hinges on the premise that the resurrected cannot be identical to the original, making just requital impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were merely a copy of your current self in the afterlife, would you feel that God's justice has truly reached you? How does that question affect your sense of personal accountability today?
?
The argument says that without identity, just requital fails. Do you live as if the 'you' that sins today is the exact same 'you' that will be judged? What would change if you truly believed that?
?
When you consider your deeds, do you ever subconsciously think that God might not hold you accountable because you will not be the same person? Confront that thought: does it come from a lack of faith in His power to re-create or from a desire to escape responsibility?

Refutation of the First Argument: The Soul and Essential Parts Survive
Taftazani refutes the first argument by denying the impossibility of re-creation and appealing to the survival of soul and essential parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's refutation of the first argument, which denies the impossibility of re-creating the identical non-existent and argues that the soul and essential parts survive, preserving identity.

Core Idea
The refutation denies that re-creation of the identical is impossible and asserts that the soul and essential parts remain, so identity is preserved.

Taftazani now responds to the first argument. He first denies the impossibility of re-creating the identical non-existent, noting that this has already been shown weak. Then he introduces a stronger point: those who believe the soul and essential parts survive can avoid the argument altogether. The foundation of desert is the soul, which remains, so the resurrected is the same person.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation carefully
Read the passage beginning with 'ورد بالمنع'. Notice how Taftazani first denies the key premise and then provides a positive alternative.
2
Identify the survival of soul and parts
Find the mention of 'بقاء الروح والأجزاء الأصلية'. Understand how this answers the argument.
The soul and essential parts are what make a person identical.
3
Consider the final answer
Taftazani concludes that the proofs of just requital are sufficient; we do not need to know the exact mechanism. How does this close the discussion?
This shows the argument is not necessary to prove.

?

Second Argument against Annihilation: Purpose Objection and Refutation
Taftazani presents and refutes the Mu'tazilite argument that annihilation lacks purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that annihilation lacks purpose and the Ash'ari refutation that God's purposes are not limited to benefit and requital.

Core Idea
God's actions need not benefit creatures; His purposes transcend human comprehension.

Taftazani presents the second argument against the view that resurrection occurs after complete annihilation. The Mu'tazila claim that a wise God cannot act without purpose, and annihilation serves no benefit or requital. This lesson unpacks that argument and its refutation, showing that God's wisdom may include purposes beyond our knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite argument
Carefully read the first part of the passage where Taftazani states the Mu'tazilite position: God must act for a purpose, and annihilation has no conceivable purpose.
Notice the key terms: فعل الحكيم, العبث, الغرض.
2
Identify the refutation
Read the refutation: the claim that purpose is limited to benefit and requital is denied. God may have other wise purposes.
Consider the examples given: announcement of annihilation as a kindness and manifestation of greatness.
3
Understand the dispersal sub-argument
The passage extends the discussion to the view that annihilation is dispersal of parts. Two issues arise: identity and purpose. Read how Taftazani answers each.
Focus on the analogy of the aging criminal for identity.
4
Reflect on the overall lesson
Ask yourself: Why do the Mu'tazila think annihilation is impossible? How does the Ash'ari refutation preserve the possibility of resurrection?
The key is that God's purposes are not limited to what humans perceive as benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - وهو للمعتزلة أن فعل الحكيم لا بد أن يكون لغرض لامتناع العبث عليه، ولا يتصور له غرض في الإعدام إذ لا منفعة فيه لأحد، لأنها إنما تكون مع الوجود، بل الحياة. وليس أيضاً جزاء المستحق كالعذاب والسؤال والحساب، ونحو ذلك، وهذا ظاهر. ورد بمنع انحصار الغرض في المنفعة والجزاء، فلعل الله تعالى في ذلك حكماً ومصالح لا يعلمها غيره على أن في الإخبار بالإعدام لطفاً للمكلفين، وإظهاراً لغاية العظمة والاستغناء، والتفرد بالدوام والبقاء، ثم الإعدام تحقيق لذلك وتصديق. وقد يورد الوجهان على طريق تفريق الأجزاء، أما الثاني فظاهر، وأما الأول فلانعدام التأليف والهيئات التي بها التمايز. فإما أن تمتنع الإعادة، أو يلتبس المعاد بالمثل ويجاب بأنه يجوز أن لا تنعدم الصفات التي بها التمايز كاختصاص الجواهر بما لها من الجهات مثلاً، ولو سلم فالمستحق هو تلك الجواهر الموصوفة الباقية لا مجموع الجواهر والصفات والتعينات، كما إذا جنى وهو شاب سمين سليم الأعضاء، واقتص منه حين صار هرماً عجيفاً ساقط الأعضاء، وعن الثاني بأن في التفريق منفعة الاعتبار، وإمكان اللذة والألم على طريق الجزاء.
The second — and it is for the Mu'tazila: that the action of the Wise must be for a purpose, because frivolity is impossible for Him, and no purpose can be conceived in annihilation, since there is no benefit in it for anyone, because benefit only occurs with existence, indeed with life. Nor is it a requital for the deserving, like punishment, questioning, reckoning, and the like. This is obvious. And it is refuted by denying that purpose is confined to benefit and requital. Perhaps God has in that wise purposes and interests that none other knows, especially since the announcement of annihilation is a kindness for the obligated, and a manifestation of ultimate greatness, self-sufficiency, and uniqueness in permanence and eternity; then annihilation is a confirmation and verification of that. And the two aspects may be raised concerning the way of dispersing the parts: as for the second, it is obvious; as for the first, it is due to the lack of composition and forms by which distinction occurs. Either re-creation becomes impossible, or the resurrected is confused with the like. And it is answered that it is possible that the attributes by which distinction occurs are not annihilated, like the specific orientation of atoms, for example. And even if it is granted, the deserving one is those persisting described atoms, not the totality of atoms, attributes, and determinations; just as if someone commits a crime while young, fat, and with sound limbs, and retaliation is taken from him when he becomes decrepit, lean, and with fallen limbs. And regarding the second, there is in dispersal a benefit of consideration, and the possibility of pleasure and pain as a way of requital.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument, attributed to the Mu'tazila, that the action of the Wise One (God) must have a purpose, because frivolity is impossible for Him. In annihilation, no purpose can be conceived: there is no benefit (since benefit requires existence, even life), and it is not a requital for the deserving (like punishment or reckoning). This is refuted by denying that purpose is limited to benefit and requital. God may have other wise purposes and interests unknown to others, especially since the announcement of annihilation is a kindness (luṭf) for the obligated, and a manifestation of God's greatness, self-sufficiency, and uniqueness in eternity; then the actual annihilation confirms and verifies that. The passage then considers applying the two aspects (the first argument about identity and the second about purpose) to the view that annihilation is actually the dispersal of parts. As for the second aspect (purpose), it is obviously fulfilled by the benefit of consideration. As for the first aspect (identity), if the parts are dispersed, the lack of composition and distinguishing forms would either make re-creation impossible or confuse the resurrected with a similar being. The answer is that the distinguishing attributes (like the specific orientation of atoms) might not be annihilated. Even if they were, the deserving entity is the persisting atoms themselves, not the aggregate of atoms, attributes, and determinations. This is analogous to a criminal who commits a crime as a young, healthy man and is punished as an old, decrepit one; the identity remains. Additionally, dispersal allows for the benefit of consideration (i.e., moral lesson) and the possibility of experiencing pleasure and pain as requital.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must always act for a purpose that directly benefits creatures or serves as requital.
Correction: God's purposes are not limited to benefit and requital; He may have wise purposes beyond human comprehension, such as manifesting His attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the Mu'tazilite argument presented here?
A) Annihilation is impossible because God cannot destroy His creation
B) God must act for a purpose, and annihilation has no conceivable purpose
C) Bodies are not annihilated but only separated
D) Resurrection is by creation after annihilation

Key Takeaway
God's actions have purposes beyond human benefit, so annihilation is not futile; His wisdom may include manifesting His attributes and providing moral lessons.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness events in your life that seem completely pointless or harmful, do you genuinely believe that God has wise purposes beyond your understanding—or do you fall into doubt? What single habit could you adopt today to affirm His wisdom in the face of confusion?
?
If you truly absorbed that God’s announcement of annihilation (and by extension, every divine decree) is a kindness for you, how would your fear of death and your trust in God change? What would you do differently this week to live in light of that truth?
?
Think of a moment when you felt your identity was shattered—a loss, a failure, a sin. The lesson teaches that your essence (your persisting atoms/soul) remains even as forms change. How can this truth anchor your sense of worth and accountability, especially when you feel utterly transformed by life’s trials?
